**Burt Bacharach – Songwriter** : A picture of the esteemed American songwriter Burt Bacharach is prominently positioned on the left side of the "Definitely Maybe" album cover. This was a direct contribution from Noel Gallagher, signifying his deep admiration and love for Bacharach's music. "Towards the front, on the left side is a picture of American songwriter Bert Bacarack. Nol loved Bacarack, so that was his contribution." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_tbKuJR0cPs
**Bush – Suggested by a Music Video Director; Named "Bush X" in Canada Due to Trademark Conflict** : When Gavin Rossdale's band was preparing to release their debut album Sixteen Stone, a music video director suggested "Bush" — short, memorable, graphical, with a mildly suggestive sexual edge. The band was then called Future Primitive. In Canada, however, an existing 1970s band named Bush held the trademark, so Rossdale's group legally traded as "Bush X" in Canada until the conflict was resolved years later. "Until that trademark issue got sorted a few years later, Bush had to legally trade under the name Bush X, but only in Canada." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QkYHaJKohVQ
**Butthole Surfers – Accidental Stage Introduction of a Song Title as a Band Name (1984)** : The stage introducer confused the band's name with the title of one of their songs at a 1984 gig. No one can now remember what name they were actually using. Previous names included The Dick Clark Five, Nine Centimeter Worm Makes Its Own Food, The Vodka Family Winstons, Ashtray Babyheads, Eadnzer's Gay, Zip Code Rapists, and several names involving Fred Astaire's anatomy. The Butthole Surfers name stuck because the gig went well, and they eventually had alt-rock radio hits. "Against all odds, they not only survived with that name, but had a couple of alt-rock radio hits." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QkYHaJKohVQ
**C86 Cassette Compilation** : In 1986, the NME (New Musical Express) resurrected the idea of offering a cassette with one of its issues, naming it C86 (a play on the year and cassette length labels like C60, C90, C120). This collection featured 22 bands from various UK indie labels, offering power pop and jangly guitar-based songs that reflected the contemporary state of new music in Britain. "They called it C86, a play on the year, 1986, and how cassette lengths were labeled. There was C60, C90, C120. That was based on the number of minutes that could be recorded on both sides." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VOv5mT1dJd8
**C86 as a Shorthand Term** : The C86 cassette proved so successful that its title became a shorthand term to describe a specific sound and attitude within a certain type of music. It showed independent musicians across the UK that they were not alone and that there was significant interest in their work, leading to an explosion of new bands, though most did not achieve widespread success. "It was so successful that C86 became a shorthand term to describe a certain sound and attitude when it came to a certain type of music." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VOv5mT1dJd8
**CBC show The Bridge - "I Got Mine" Theme** : The Black Keys' song "I Got Mine" was selected and used as the opening theme for the CBC television series *The Bridge*. "Remember the CBC show The Bridge? That's the opening theme." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=PmwfC4HR3_I
**CBGB's Full Name and "Gorman Dising"** : The famous punk bar CBGB in New York has a full and proper name: "CBGB on Fug," which stands for "Country, Bluegrass, Blues, and other music for uplifting Gorman Dizers." This term "Gorman Dising" was speculated as a possible anagrammatic origin for the band name "Imagine Dragons," although the band has never confirmed it. "The full and proper name of that club is CBGB on Fug. And that honestly stands for Country, Bluegrass, Blues, and other music for uplifting Gorman Dizers." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GmYlTwI_TQQ
**CD Adoption Accelerated by Vinyl Quality Decline** : While superior audio technology like the CD would eventually prevail, the rapid adoption of compact discs starting in late 1982 was significantly accelerated by the severe decline in vinyl quality throughout the 1970s and early 1980s. The poor manufacturing standards of vinyl records created a strong consumer demand for a higher-fidelity alternative. "But you have to wonder if we would have got the compact disc as soon as we did, and if we would have all adopted them so quickly, had vinyl quality been up to snuff through the 1970s and early 80s." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NAroxFbYkRw
**CD Player Skip Button's Impact** : The "skip button" on CD players profoundly affected listener attention spans and tolerance levels for music. It accustomed listeners to quickly bypass disliked tracks, influencing how entire albums are consumed. "Think about what the skip button on CD players did for our attention spans and tolerances when it came to music." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-CT7yYXonIk
**CD Players' Impact on Album Integrity** : The advent of CD players and their skip functionality marked the initial erosion of the album's integrity, enabling listeners to bypass disliked songs or even reorder tracks, rather than experiencing the album as a continuous artistic statement. "This was the first dent in the wall for the integrity of the album." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**CD Ripping** : The advent of MP3 technology and the subsequent release of its source code by a hacker led to a surge in "CD ripping." This allowed individuals to easily convert physical CDs into digital files, which could then be shared freely over the internet, further disrupting the traditional music industry. "Things could even worse when a hacker found the source code. He created an even better version, set it free on the internet, and well, CD ripping went wild." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VnXBJqKwkrw
**CD Single Phasing Out in North America** : The phasing out of the CD single in North America contributed to fan frustration. It meant that to acquire a single track, even from a one-hit wonder, consumers often had to purchase an entire album costing $18 or more, despite promises of falling CD prices. "the CD single was being phased out of North America. That meant that even if you did like a song put up by one of those one-hit wonders, you had to pony up 18 bucks or more just to get that one track." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-CT7yYXonIk
**CFNY Film Documentary** : A documentary film covering "the spirit of radio CFNY years," spanning from 1977 to 1992, is currently in its final editing stages. The production involved extensive interviews in locations such as London, Glasgow, Manchester, Liverpool (including with Peter Hook), Abbey Road Studios, Vancouver, San Francisco, Los Angeles, and Las Vegas. The documentary aims to explore how the radio station introduced music to the masses and influenced the growth of alternative music in North America. "We are doing a documentary, a film documentary on the spirit of radio CF and Y years. So that would be 1977 to 1992 and we're in the final editing stages right now." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=4xxgPX-aR90
**COVID-19 Pandemic's Acceleration of Digital Music Transition** : The COVID-19 pandemic, causing a two-year halt in live music (concerts, festivals), severely impacted artists and venues. It forced a rapid acceleration of the music industry's digital transition, leading to a surge in virtual concerts, live streams, and the explosion of TikTok for music discovery. Revenue shifted to crowdfunding, merchandise, and platforms like Patreon, while streaming services like Spotify, Apple Music, and YouTube saw massive user spikes. While some artists created "COVID music" about isolation and resilience, widespread home production using similar software led to a "flattening of music." Post-pandemic, ticket prices surged due to inflation, and logistical issues like tour bus shortages emerged. Despite its devastation, COVID-19 significantly propelled the industry and fan consumption deeper into the digital realm. "COVID-19 was unspeakably awful. But it did accelerate the music industry's transition into the digital realm, and the effects will be felt not only on the industry, but also on music fans." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nX8uAFDzDYM
**COVID-19's Influence on Creative Process** : The COVID-19 pandemic, despite its challenges, unexpectedly sparked new creativity for bands like 5440. Forced to connect remotely via Zoom calls, band members shared historical stories and anecdotes, which directly became the "impetus" for writing new songs and forming the basis of an upcoming album. "Brad came up with the idea of, you know, doing a Zoom call... this became the impetus, which is for the record that's going to come out next, because we just started telling stories and, you know, fun and funny stuff about our history." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=W9gdKha0PLg
**Cage the Elephant – Multiple Conflicting Stories; Band Won't Tell** : The band offers conflicting origin stories — a mentally deranged man yelling "you gotta cage the elephant" at them, a fortune teller turning over a card and screaming it. They've said, "We'll never tell." "There's a bunch of others, the band's coy." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NCQ1ERgSfeE
**Cake – Not About Pastry; About Something That Sticks to Your Shoe** : Formed in Sacramento in 1991, Cake chose their name to convey something that "insidiously becomes part of your life" — the way something cakes onto the bottom of your shoe. "Cake onto your shoe, and it's just sort of there until you get rid of it." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QkYHaJKohVQ
**Calabasas Celebrity Enclave** : Calabasas, California, is described as a "gated enormous" community and celebrity hub, where figures like Will Smith, Justin Bieber, and Travis Barker reside. John Mayer's old mansion in this area served as a studio for USS to write songs like "Who's With Me." "That's what we'll Smith lives that's where Justin Bieber lives that's where Travis Barker lives." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=N-3eddy4J7g
**Callie – Cranberries Art Director** : Callie was the art director hired for The Cranberries' 1993 debut album, "Everybody Else Is Doing It, So Why Can't We?" Her role was instrumental in establishing the band's early visual aesthetic, including actively seeking out and reusing the same couch for their subsequent album, "No Need to Argue," and its reappearance in 2017. "An art director called Callie was hired." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_tbKuJR0cPs
**CanCon Controversies and Edge Cases – Dallas Green + Pink, Pop Evil, Arcade Fire** : Dallas Green of Alexis on Fire and City and Colour collaborated with Pink on a 2014 album called "You and Me." Because files were exchanged between home studios across countries, a CRTC ruling was needed. The album received CanCon certification because the CRTC ruled Green's contribution as a lead artist was sufficient for a full "A" — but this came retroactively, after the album was already released. Pop Evil, a Michigan band with a Canadian founder (Lee Kelly), also required a CRTC ruling on whether their songs qualified. Arcade Fire has American-born members but qualifies as a Canadian act because they are landed immigrants. "You do not want to screw up your cancon numbers." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-JWO_jYXwEM
**CanCon Raised to 35% on January 3, 1999; Most New Stations Promise 40%** : After 20+ years of development, the Canadian music scene was strong enough that when quotas were raised from 30 to 35% on January 3, 1999, "almost nobody noticed." Since 2000, most new radio station applications have voluntarily promised a 40% CanCon quota — evidence that programmers believe such a ratio works with audiences. Canada is the fifth or sixth largest music market in the world, regularly competing with Australia for that ranking, always behind the US, Japan, UK, Germany, and France. "One of us is the US, Japan, the UK, Germany and France, all of which have larger populations than us." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-JWO_jYXwEM
**CanCon's Industrial Impact – Labels, Recording Studios, and Infrastructure Created by Mandate** : By creating artificial demand for Canadian music, the CanCon rules required the creation of a domestic music industry infrastructure: recording studios, producers, engineers, promoters, booking agents, and record labels. Within a year of the rules taking effect, new Canadian labels appeared including GRT, Attic, Stony Plain, Aquarius, Nimbus 9, Axe, and Daffodil. Initially, Canadian records were often technically and artistically inferior; stations created so-called "Beaver Hours," playing heavily edited Canadian songs between 11 p.m. and midnight to meet quotas in one concentrated block. The CRTC closed that loophole. "For a while, Canadian meant bad." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-JWO_jYXwEM
**Canada's Reaction to Gord's Diagnosis** : The news of Gord Downie's incurable brain tumor, announced on May 24, 2016, had a profound impact across Canada, becoming the lead story everywhere and hitting the nation "like a bomb." "This news hit Canada like a bomb. Lead story everywhere." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Um_KEEcCeT4
**Canadian Broadcast Standards Council (CBSC)** : This council became involved when an unedited version of The Headstones' song "Cubically Contained," containing clear profanity, was broadcast on radio at 5:30 PM. The CBSC received complaints from listeners, particularly those concerned about children, highlighting its role in overseeing broadcast content and standards. "Then there's something called the Canadian Brock East Anders Council. And of course a bunch of people were listing very concerned, you know, what about the children?" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=y7kmkWJ54ww
**Canadian Content Laws (January 1971)** : Introduced in January 1971, these laws mandated that Canadian radio stations allocate 30% of their playlists to Canadian artists. This created an artificial demand for domestic music, which, despite initial opposition, stimulated the growth of a modern Canadian music industry with expanded infrastructure, allowing Canadian artists to build successful careers within the country. "But then came the Canadian content laws in January 1971, and overnight it became law that Canadian radio stations had to devote 30% of their playlists to Canadian artists." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=P0pOgz3ZobE
**Canadian Content Regulations (CanCon) – January 18, 1971; 30% Canadian Music Required on AM Radio** : On January 18, 1971, the Canadian Radio Television and Communications Commission (CRTC) mandated that 30% of all music heard on Canadian AM radio stations had to be of Canadian origin. The regulation was developed largely through lobbying by Stan Klees and Walt Grealis, co-founders of RPM Magazine (established Toronto, 1964), Canada's first domestic music industry trade publication tracking radio and charts. Grealis also conceived the Gold Leaf Awards in 1970 (renamed the Juno Awards in 1971, named after CRTC head Pierre Juneau), which began as an annual column with a small wine and cheese party for 250 people. The first Juno Awards took place February 22, 1971. "It turned Canada into a global musical powerhouse eventually." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-JWO_jYXwEM
**Canadian FM Radio Regulations (Decades prior to 1990s)** : For decades, FM radio in Canada was heavily regulated to maintain its distinctiveness from AM radio. Regulations included a ban on playing the same song more than 18 times a week and a limit of 49% of a playlist dedicated to top 40 records. FM stations were also mandated to meet "stringent talk requirements," categorized into surveillance (weather, traffic), mosaic talk (60+ seconds on a specific topic), news in public affairs, and foreground programming (self-contained shows at least 30 minutes long on specific topics). These foreground programming requirements were eventually "ditched sometime in the 1990s." "For decades, FM Radio in Canada was highly, highly regulated." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=bI6TTA-fn7c
**Canadian Music Industry Maturation (1971-1990s)** : The Canadian music industry underwent a roughly 20-year maturation process, evolving into a world-class entity by the 1990s, at which point Canadian artists were perceived as capable of competing globally. "It took about 20 years for our music industry to mature into something truly world class. And by the time we got to the 1990s, there was this sense that our best could compete with anyone or anything in the world." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=P0pOgz3ZobE
**Canadian Music Industry Pre-1971** : Prior to 1971, Canada's music industry was considered a "backwater," lacking the infrastructure of a "first world industry." Major labels maintained only branch offices, and aspiring artists typically had to move to the United States to achieve significant success. "Before 1971, there really wasn't much of a Canadian music industry. Canada was basically a backwater, a place where the big labels had branch offices." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=P0pOgz3ZobE
**Canadian Polymer Coins Can Be Used as a Record Needle to Play Music Very Faintly** : Canadian polymer coins are thin enough that they can function as a stylus on a rotating record player, reproducing music very faintly. "Canadian polymer coins are so thin they can be used as a needle to play music very faintly on a turntable." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=qtdetc5-MQo
**Canadian Radio "F-Bomb" Rule (French Language)** : A Canadian regulatory body has ruled that the use of the "F-bomb" is permissible on Canadian radio, provided it is broadcast in French. The justification is that the word has become very common in the French language, and its use by a French speaker on radio is considered less offensive than if spoken in English. "A regulatory body has ruled it's okay to use the F-bomb on Canadian radio as long as it's broadcast in French." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aSk1XQHNkd8
**Capital Radio UK (October 16, 1973) – First Private Commercial Radio Station in Britain; Punks Couldn't Get Airplay** : Until October 16, 1973, all legal radio broadcasting in the UK came from the publicly funded BBC. That day, Capital FM signed on at 95.8 FM with 557 AM as the country's first private commercial station. For years Capital played safe mainstream pop. When punk emerged, both the BBC and Capital refused to play it, infuriating The Clash, who wrote a song explicitly about their frustration. "That greatly annoyed the Clash, who couldn't get airplay anywhere." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JvOEAzmC6l0
**Capitol Records Building – Flashing "Hollywood" in Morse Code Since 1956** : The iconic Capitol Records building in Los Angeles is a 13-story circular structure topped with a needle-like flashing light. That light has been blinking out the word "Hollywood" in Morse code continuously since the building opened in 1956. "It has been blinking out Hollywood in Morse code since it opened in 1956." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fnZx8EGnszw
**Car Radio Ban Attempt (1930s) – Massachusetts Registrar Tried to Outlaw In-Car Radios** : The first car radio appeared in 1930 as the Motorola 5T71, a delicate vacuum-tube device that could add as much as $120 to a $600 car. George A. Parker, the registrar of motor vehicles in Massachusetts, campaigned to ban radios in cars on distracted driving grounds, coming close to having his way. The public rose up against him and shut him down; Parker redirected his efforts toward drunk driving. "The good citizens of Massachusetts rose up as one and slathered him down." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=iyGUXdDqIlI
**Car Seat Headrest – Singer Will Toledo Rehearsed Vocals in the Back Seat of the Family Car** : At 17, Will Toledo needed a quiet, soundproofed space to rehearse. The best available was the back seat of the family car, staring at the front headrests. "He rehearsed his vocals in the most quiet and most soundproofed place he knew, the back seat of the family car." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QkYHaJKohVQ
**Carl Heinz Brandenburg: MP3 Algorithm Development (1991-1995)** : Carl Heinz Brandenburg and his team at the Fraunhofer Institute pioneered the development of the MP3 algorithm, establishing the mathematical principles by 1991. Their research focused on how the human brain selectively perceives music, enabling them to strip out "invisible sound" from audio files. This process reduced file sizes to about one-tenth of the original, making audio transmission over phone lines significantly faster and easier. The algorithm was refined and accepted as an international standard by 1994, becoming widely available by 1995, coincident with the internet's public emergence. "Brennenberg and his crew worked out the math by 1991. By 1994 it had been refined and accepted as an international standard. By 1995 it was in the wild." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=PNiL63fY1Y8
**Cassette Tape Recording Format** : Cassettes were a widely used analog magnetic tape recording format designed for both audio recording and playback. Although the technology began to emerge in the 1960s, its peak cultural impact and widespread adoption as a dominant medium for audio consumption occurred throughout the 1970s and 1980s. "Cassettes were an analog magnetic tape recording format for audio recording and playback, although the birth and growth of the cassette began in the 1960s, its cultural moment took place during the 1970s and 1980s." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZleIqEpaNio
**Cassette Technology's Flaws** : Cassettes are described as an "old, inferior technology" whose attempted resurrection is misguided due to inherent flaws. They deliver poor sound quality, are prone to jamming, and restrict mix tape creation to real-time. Over time, the glue binding magnetic particles to the tape dries out, leading to degradation and the "forever irreparable" loss of music information. Additionally, their plastic boxes have fragile hinges, and cassettes frequently end up damaged in car footwells or melt on dashboards, further compounded by the absence of factory cassette players in modern automobiles. "Cossets are an old, inferior technology that served us well in their day and serve no purpose whatsoever." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7Zniri9uSlc
**Catalog Sale as Accelerated Future Royalties** : The money artists receive from selling their catalog is essentially an accelerated payout of royalties they would have earned over time anyway. This means they get a large lump sum upfront rather than waiting for smaller, periodic royalty checks. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sfie4YYncxk
**Catalog Sales for Younger Artists** : Catalog sales are also becoming attractive to younger artists, offering financial freedom despite giving up future royalties on older songs. They can still earn from new music and use the substantial cash influx for philanthropy, non-musical investments, activism, or estate planning, eliminating financial worry about new album success. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sfie4YYncxk
**Caudate and Musical Anticipation** : The caudate, a specific sub-region within the brain, possesses the remarkable ability to anticipate the emotional climax of a song up to 15 seconds before it actually occurs. This predictive capacity is why songs that progressively build intensity before a significant drop or release evoke such a strong, pleasurable sensation, as the brain instinctively senses a forthcoming "big payoff." "the co-date can somehow anticipate the emotional climax of a song up to 15 seconds before it happens." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mTbagQE7akQ
**Celebrity Skin and Body Fluids – Company Selling Rock Star Bacteria, Skin Cells, and Fecal Matter** : A company called Celebrity Skin and Body Fluids, founded anonymously by former Hollywood personal assistants with access to their employers' private spaces, offered for sale biological material attributed to various rock musicians. Documented inventory included: Dave Navarro's personal bacteria at $5.75 per batch; Michael Stipe of R.E.M.'s dead skin cells at $12.75; Flea of the Red Hot Chili Peppers' fecal matter at $27 per cubic inch; and Billy Corgan of the Smashing Pumpkins' fecal matter at $30 per cubic inch (priced higher due to scarcity). "Why would anyone want any of this stuff? Beyond me, unless of course you were engaged in some kind of weird cloning experiment." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=lyYsTon_NSc
**Challenge of Song Identification in Streaming** : A critical component for the streaming payment system is the precise identification of each played song, which presents an extremely complex challenge to eliminate all potential errors in attributing plays. "How do you identify a specific song so that you've eliminated all possibility of error?" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**Challenges for Musicians Post-Original Band** : After experiencing the creation and performance of their own original music and albums in a band, it becomes extremely difficult for musicians to transition back to being a "working musician" who solely plays other people's songs. "Once you've done that you can't. It's very hard to go back to being like a working musician that just playing in the music." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=zBd13KtYlAA
**Challenges for New Musicians in the Internet Era** : Making it in the music business has always been difficult, requiring not just talent but also significant luck to be in the right place at the right time with the right sound, image, and attitude. Since the internet disrupted the industry around 2000 with free digital files, streaming, social media, and YouTube, it has become even harder for new acts to be heard above the exponentially increased competition and noise. "It has never been harder for a new act to be heard above the noise of everyone else." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GmYlTwI_TQQ
**Challenges for Post-2000 Artists** : Artists emerging after 2000 face significant struggles in building careers due to an overwhelming volume of music, intense competition, and excessive choice for music fans, making it exceptionally difficult to gain recognition in the post-internet era. "But artists that have risen after 2000, let's generally be in a struggle, largely because it's almost impossible to make a name for yourself in the post-internet era." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**Challenges for Struggling Musicians** : Struggling performers, who form a vast underclass, are forced to spend much of their time on non-creative activities to make ends meet, making it difficult for them to develop, experiment, and mature creatively into something great. "They spend so much of their time doing things other than creating music that it's a challenge to be creative and to experiment and to develop and mature into something great." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z7glrAoXtkA
**Challenges of Band Longevity** : Maintaining a band's lineup over many years presents significant challenges, including the constant close proximity of members, cramped conditions on tour, record company issues, personal issues, and the general fragility of human relationships. Despite these difficulties, some bands, like Billy Talent, Radiohead, U2, and ZZ Top, manage to maintain a consistent lineup for decades. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ocMHRLQMQY0
**Chanel Glove – Photography Prop** : A black Chanel glove, which was a leftover prop from a previous photoshoot, was spontaneously utilized by photographer Colin Lane for the controversial international cover of The Strokes' "Is This It." The glove adorned the hand of his then-girlfriend in the striking image. "There were some props left around from a previous shoot, including that Chanel glove." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_tbKuJR0cPs
**Changes in Song Intros** : The average instrumental lead-up to vocals in a pop song has drastically shortened from about 17 seconds in the 1980s to less than five seconds today, a direct consequence of the need to immediately engage listeners in the streaming era. "Now in the 80s, the average instrumental lead up to the vocals of a pop song was around 17 seconds. Now it's less than five." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z7glrAoXtkA
**Changing Attitudes to Music Possession** : Modern attitudes towards music ownership are shifting, with less emphasis on physical possession. The ability to access any song on demand from "the cloud" reduces the perceived need to personally own the music, especially with seamless internet access in environments like cars. "If I can access any song I want any time I feel like it, who says I actually have to have that music on my person? Why can't I just get it from the cloud that mysterious part of the internet that has the ability to store anything?" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-CT7yYXonIk
**Changing Metrics for Superstardom** : Traditional metrics like album sales and chart positions are considered "so 20th century" and are no longer reliable indicators of an act's popularity due to drastic changes in the music industry since 2000. "The music industry has changed drastically since 2000, so we need different metrics. Album sales and chart positions are, you know, so 20th century." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z7glrAoXtkA
**Changing Role of Radio and Music Television (Mid-2000s)** : The traditional support for bands from outlets like MuchMusic began to wane in the mid-2000s, as networks started shifting older-generation bands to less prominent or specialized channels (e.g., "MuchLOUD"). This indicated a changing landscape where established acts were no longer considered "cool" enough for mainstream rotation, impacting their visibility and airplay. "much music isn't going to play. They're throwing you over too much light or whatever the hell it was called. Don't worry, Blue rodeos there too. You know, that kind of things like what? What? We're not cool anymore." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=W9gdKha0PLg
**Chanie Wenjack's Story** : Chanie Wenjack was a young Indigenous boy caught in the residential school system. He was sent to a residential school in Kenora, Ontario, from which he ran away. He tragically attempted to walk 600 kilometers back home but died of exposure alongside a railway track, a story that profoundly inspired Gord Downie's "Secret Path" project and the Chanie Wenjack Fund. "Cheney-Wenjack was caught up in the whole residential school's travesty. He was sent to Canora, Ontario, but he ran away. And he tried to walk the 600 kilometers home, but he'd never made it, dying of exposure alongside a railway track." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Um_KEEcCeT4
**Charles Dickens' Book Tour and Scalping (1867)** : A situation similar to Jenny Lind's ticket scandal occurred in 1867 during Charles Dickens' book tour of America. Tickets for his readings immediately vanished from box offices but were readily available from "sidewalk men," illustrating the pervasive nature of ticket scalping even for non-musical events. "A similar situation arose in 1867 when Charles Dickens went on a book tour of America." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=D8GU8ZpSgY8
**Charles Manson TikTok Virality (2022) – "Home Is Where You're Happy" (1970) Used in Autumn Videos** : For unknown reasons, a 1970 song by Charles Manson entitled "Home Is Where You're Happy" went viral on TikTok in 2022, used by hundreds of creators as the soundtrack to videos celebrating the fall season. "Which just seems wrong." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZbHRIh3RUoo
**Chart Achievement - First Song to Reach Number One on Three Charts** : A song achieved a unique chart milestone by being the first ever to reach number one simultaneously on three distinct charts: Alternative Songs, Mainstream Rock Songs, and Adult Alternative Songs. This was a significant accomplishment, highlighting its broad appeal across different rock formats. "Plus, it was the first song to reach number one on three charts. Alternative songs, mainstream rock songs, and adult alternative songs." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=MmXDbXI6Sqk
**Chateau Marmont Hotel - Iconic LA Location** : The Chateau Marmont Hotel on Sunset Boulevard in Los Angeles is a notable location mentioned multiple times, serving as Courtney Love's residence at one point and later as the venue for the after-party of the "Who the F Is Arthur Fogel" documentary premiere, attended by Lady Gaga and Stewart Copeland. "After the screening, there was an after party in the bar at the Chateau Marmont, that place again." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hjProJd_JrM
**Chateau Marmont Hotel – Sunset Boulevard** : This hotel on Sunset Boulevard served as a temporary refuge for Scott Wyland when his wife refused to let him come home in May 1995, where he sought solace with Courtney Love. It was also where Wyland recorded some of his solo material, like "Chateau Mars," on a four-track recorder. "Scott Wyland recorded at the Chateau Marmont Hotel on sunset bullifard." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TDwagoyMvck
**Chechnya Banned Music Outside 80–115 BPM by Government Decree (2024)** : In April 2024, the Chechen government decreed that all music played in the republic must fall between 80 and 115 beats per minute, to ensure that musical and dance creations "align with the Chechen mentality and musical rhythm." All music too fast or too slow was banned. "All music in Chechnya must fall within between 80 and 115 beats per minute." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fnZx8EGnszw
**Cheese Maturation with Music** : A cheesemaker in Bergedorf, Switzerland, named Beat Womfleur, experiments with bombarding his maturing Emmenthal cheese with various genres of music, ranging from Led Zeppelin to Techno. He believes this process enhances the cheese's taste, although he lacks scientific data to support his claim. "To do that, he bombardes his maturing cheese with music from Led Zeppelin to Techno." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GO0K_N7Ys7I
**Chelsea Drug Raid (September 1996)** : In September 1996, her house in Chelsea was raided, leading to the discovery of a sizable quantity of opium and smoking pipes. He found a sizable quantity of opium and a number of smoking pipes stashed at Paulus House in Chelsea." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=23OgreyK66o

His death was particularly shocking and unexpected, even amidst a period of many musician deaths around that time. "By the end of the day, everything was confirmed. "eight children of people who've worked on the tour in the past were themselves working on the tour as of 2014." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7jfQaUV6Bq4
**Chinese Government Censorship of Hip Hop Culture** : The Chinese government has actively suppressed hip hop, deeming it rebellious and contrary to the principles of the Chinese Communist Party. This suppression includes an order for all TV producers to blur the earlobes of hip hop performers wearing earrings, and similar censorship applies to tattoos, as part of an effort to eliminate perceived "immoral culture." "The government has ordered all TV producers to blur out the earlobes of all hip hop performers." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HoBx6s3JmVQ
**Chinese Pop Fans Cause Food Shortage and Show Cancellation (2021)** : In China, a boy band creation program sponsored by a dairy company offered fans extra voting power for scanning QR codes on milk bottle caps. Fans orchestrated an online campaign to purchase millions of dollars worth of the milk solely for the caps, then poured the liquid down the drain, leading to a food shortage. This widespread food waste outraged authorities, who responded by implementing new anti-food waste laws and canceling the boy band show. "Fans created an online drive to buy millions of dollars worth of this drink just for the bottle caps." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-N6IM5Nm5iw
**Chris Hadfield Zero G Video (2013)** : Prior to Ok Go's atmospheric Zero G music video, Commander Chris Hadfield released a Zero G video aboard the International Space Station in 2013. This provided a benchmark for similar creative endeavors in microgravity environments. "And before you point out that Commander Chris Hadfield did a Zero G video aboard the International Space Station in 2013, you are right." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hmnMG6f80Sg
**Circle Pits and Wall of Death** : As slam dancing evolved, larger gatherings at gigs saw the formation of "circles of slam dancers" at various points in the crowd. These intense formations became known as circle pits, and sometimes escalated into the "wall of death," where two sides of the pit would charge at each other. "These became known as circle pits and sometimes the wall of death." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=KMx4bmq8o2Y
**City and Colour – Dallas Green; City = Dallas; Colour = Green** : "Terribly obvious yet not." Dallas Green. Get it? "City and color, which is terribly obvious yet not." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NCQ1ERgSfeE
**Classification of Musicians (Homo Blatodia)** : A humorous, non-scientific classification suggests a new subspecies, Homo Blatodia, named after the cockroach genus. Within this, two lineages are proposed: "periplaneta, philogenosa, otherwise known as the smokey brown cockroach" to classify Keith Richards, and "periplaneta americana, the American cockroach" to classify John Frusciante, owing to his perceived indestructibility despite a hard life. "And if I'm sitting with my taxonomy flow chart, this is where I write the name John Fruciante, the, let's call it, occasional guitarist of the red hot chili peppers." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TESrEzVHLCc
**Clement Adler's Pioneering Streaming** : In 1881, French engineer Clement Adler demonstrated the Tiatrophone, a system that effectively "streamed" live opera and symphony performances from Parisian stages to remote locations via telephone lines. His work predated modern streaming by over a century and established the foundational concept of delivering live audio remotely. "So can we say that Clement Adler was the inventor of streaming music? Sure, why not?" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VnXBJqKwkrw
**Clyde Stubblefield's Recognition and Support** : Unlike Gregory Coleman, Clyde Stubblefield received some recognition, including various awards and a set of autographed sticks in the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame, despite not getting credit or royalties for his "Funky Drummer" break. Prince even paid tens of thousands for his healthcare, and he received an honorary doctorate before his death in 2017. "Clyde fared a lot better than Gregory Coleman. He received no credits on the James Brown song, nor did he get any royalties from the hundreds of times his playing was sampled." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VnXBJqKwkrw
**Clyde Stubblefield's Unsung Status** : Despite significant recognition from within the music community, including praise from artists and induction into the Rock and Roll Hall of Fame for his influential "Funky Drummer" break, Clyde Stubblefield's widespread public recognition remained limited. He shaped music profoundly, "albeit unintentionally and very much uncompensated." "Yet you got to wonder how many people outside of the music community know of Clyde's Stubblefield and the incredible effect he had on shaping music with that one drum brick, albeit unintentionally and very much uncompensated." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VnXBJqKwkrw
**Cocaine and Audio Perception** : During the recording of *Be Here Now*, it was noted that cocaine consumption can negatively affect one's hearing and audio perception, influencing production decisions such as Noel Gallagher's insistence on making the record sound "bigger and louder" with excessive layers of guitars and boosted high frequencies. "Cocaine messes with your hearing and your audio perception." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=S6qB5B1PdyA
**Coffee and Post-Concert Hearing Impairment** : Following exposure to high volumes of music at a concert which can cause temporary hearing impairment, consuming coffee is ill-advised. The combination of caffeine and audio overstimulation has been shown to extend the duration required for hearing to return to its normal state. "Caffeine plus audio overstimulation equals more time for your hearing to get back to normal." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mTbagQE7akQ
**Coldplay X&Y Cover (2005) – Baudot Code; Supposed to Say "X and Y" but Actually Reads "X96" Due to Encoding Error** : Coldplay hired design company Tappin Gofton for X&Y after liking their work on the Chemical Brothers' Push the Button. Tappin Gofton researched Baudot code — a binary telegraph code invented in 1874 by Emil Baudot as International Telegraph Alphabet Number One — and designed the cover using colored blocks representing Baudot encoded letters. The colors were their addition; original Baudot code was black and white. The design was intended to spell out "X and Y." However, somebody got the encoding wrong — to a Baudot expert, the cover actually reads "X96." "To a Baudoe code pro, the album is actually apparently called X96." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JdSUHkXM1mQ
**Coldplay – Adopted from Another Band Who Had the Name from a Poetry Collection** : Originally called Starfish at university. Another Oxford group called Coldplay (two words) had taken their name from a poetry collection called "Children's Reflections Coldplay." That band was going defunct and agreed to give up the name. "That other band were in the process of going defunct so Starfish asked them if they could have their name." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NCQ1ERgSfeE
**Colin Lane – The Strokes Photographer** : Colin Lane was the photographer who captured the image for the controversial international cover of The Strokes' 2001 debut album, "Is This It." The photograph, featuring his then-girlfriend's naked rear and hip with a black Chanel glove, was a spontaneous shot taken as she stepped out of the shower. Lane has maintained the anonymity of the woman. "That shot was taken by Colin Lane of his girlfriend." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_tbKuJR0cPs
**Collaborative, Data-Driven Songwriting** : Modern songwriting, driven by audience engagement data, often requires the combined efforts of multiple musicians, beatmakers, producers, and lyricists to craft a single song optimized for streaming success. "The middies of musicians and beatmakers and producers and lyricists are needed to write a single song using audience engagement data." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**Collapse of Recorded Music Sales (2000-2009)** : Between 2000 and 2009, sales of recorded music experienced a dramatic collapse after decades of growth. CD sales were cut in half globally, with the U.S. market dropping 52% and annual sales plummeting from nearly a billion discs to 374 million by 2009. "Between 2000 and the start of 2009, CD sales were cut in half in just about every territory around the world." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-CT7yYXonIk
**Collectibility of Rare Cassettes** : Certain rare cassette releases, particularly those from influential artists or early solo projects, can become highly collectible. For instance, a copy of Dave Grohl's solo album *Pocketwatch*, released as "Late" on a cassette-only label, can trade for "$700 or more" in good condition due to its rarity and historical significance. "A copy of an album called Pocketwatch and Good Condition trades for $700 or more." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_AY5lS-FmlU
**College Radio's Growing Power** : Though listenership wasn't huge, campus stations became increasingly organized under national and international associations, with conferences, magazines, and their own music charts. This organization significantly amplified college radio's power within the broader music industry. "Now listenership wasn't huge, but as campus stations became more organized under national and international associations with conferences and magazines and even their own music charts, college radio became more and more powerful in the general scheme of the music industry." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sCtQqVBtCaI
**College Radio's Rise and Influence** : By the mid-1980s, college radio became a highly important and influential factor in music, welcoming indie bands that commercial radio and MTV largely ignored. These stations, not dependent on ratings for revenue, could be more daring and experimental, making them the primary source for the newest and most exciting music for many. "By the mid-80s, college radio was becoming a very important and very influential factor in music. While most commercial radio stations were unwilling to play anything that wasn't on a major label and on MTV, indie bands were welcomed with open arms on campus and nonprofit public radio." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sCtQqVBtCaI
**College Rock Network and Charts** : As the 1980s progressed, a network developed among campus radio stations in the US and Canada that played college rock. Airplay was tracked, leading to the creation of charts that provided valuable data. This data was then utilized by bands and their labels for strategic decisions, such as planning tours. "Eventually, a network developed between these campus radio stations. Airplay was tracked, which led to charts, and these charts provided data used by the bands and their labels to determine things like tours." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LgJ7ww2AgF4
**Collision of Music and Technology** : The proliferation of smartphones is predicted to have a transformative impact on music and society in the next decade, akin to the transistor radio's influence in the 1950s and 60s. Constant internet connectivity will make entertainment, information, and communication universally accessible through touchscreens. Previous technological shifts, such as MP3 players, drastically altered public interaction by promoting isolation through headphones, while cell phones have changed private conversations into often one-sided public broadcasts. "the proliferation of smartphones are going to be to the next 10 years with the transistor radio west to the 50s and 60s." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Yl2poVWguu0
**Columbia's LP (June 1948) – Polyvinyl Chloride from Sewer Pipe Industry; Microgrooves; 24 Minutes Per Side** : Columbia Records introduced the long-playing album in June 1948 using polyvinyl chloride (PVC), which had first gone into use in the 1920s for sewer pipes. PVC was far more durable than the shellac used for 78s and allowed grooves to be placed much closer together using a tiny stylus — Columbia called these microgrooves. A standard 78 held a spiral about 270 feet long (roughly 4 minutes per side); the new LP had a spiral over 1,600 feet long, yielding up to 24 minutes of music per side. LP stands for Long Playing. "Columbia picked up on the older technology as they were experimenting with the new substance called polyvinyl chloride, which first went into use in the 1920s for sewer pipes." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=I291j5WBc4k
**Columbine Killings (1999) and Marilyn Manson** : The Columbine killings in Colorado on April 20, 1999, led to significant public blame placed on Marilyn Manson, reportedly because the two perpetrators were fans of his music. This association resulted in Manson being unwelcome in Colorado for several years. "The South African samurai slasherns evoked memories of the Columbine killings in Colorado on April 20, 1999." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FtGJHhqXcDI
**Columbine Shooting and Goth Vilification** : The Columbine High School shooting, where perpetrators Eric and Dylan were linked to Marilyn Manson's music, "did more to vilify the whole notion of Goth than any other in the history of this music." "This one event did more to vilify the whole notion of Goth than any other in the history of this music." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=evlGhfUe6QQ
**Coming of Age with Music** : The period of "coming of age" with music typically begins in high school and concludes upon reaching adulthood (roughly ages 14 to 24). During this time, music plays a central role in self-discovery, bonding with like-minded individuals, and projecting one's identity to the world. "We all go through a period of coming of age with music. This is the period in our lives when music is central to everything we do. We use music to figure out who we are. We use it to bond with other like-minded people. And we use music to project our identity to the world. We use it to say, this is who I am. That period, and I am generalizing here, begins when we enter high school and ends when we get around to becoming adults. So that's roughly 10 years, ages 14 to 24, plus or minus a couple of years." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=v_am7ifh7Pk
**Common Two-Sibling Bands** : While the focus of this exploration is on larger family units, many bands commonly feature two siblings, such as Oasis, Radiohead, Jesus and Mary Chain, Good Charlotte, and 30 Seconds to Mars (brothers); Tegan and Sara and The Breeders (sisters); and No Doubt, Broods, and The Carpenters (brother-sister combinations). "Okay, there were plenty of bands featuring two brothers. Okay, Oasis, Radiohead, Jesus Marychain, Good Charlotte, 30 seconds to Mars, and lots of groups featuring sisters like Teagan and Sarah, the breeders, and a chunk of brother sister outfits, like no doubt, broods and I guess the Carpenters." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=gxbuajGKbko
**Compact Cassette (Philips, 1963) – Democratized Tape Trading** : The compact cassette, invented by Philips in 1963, miniaturized magnetic tape technology and made it portable and affordable, triggering an explosion in tape-trading culture that would sustain the bootleg community through the pre-internet era. "Made tape technology portable and accessible, greatly boosting the 'tape trading culture.'" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=OrHtPVBjXw4
**Comparison of 1972 vs. 1982 Record Quality** : Records pressed in 1972 were generally thicker, heavier, and of superior physical quality compared to those pressed just ten years later, around 1982, reflecting the economic and material changes that impacted vinyl production during that decade. "Now it's really weird because a record press in 1972 is often thicker, heavier, and of better physical quality than a record press ten years later." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=IIJySSo276k
**Competition from Entire Musical History** : Current artists now face unprecedented competition, not just from contemporaries but from the entire musical history of the human race, which is instantly accessible via streaming. This makes it far more difficult for new acts to "break through" and achieve widespread consensus for superstar status. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=eqTFinLk3oU
**Complexity of Rider Negotiation and Enforcement** : Crafting a list of requests in a rider that can be consistently enforced night after night, across various venues, cities, and countries, is described as a "delicate and essential thing," with some bands being more adept at this than others. "Coming up with a list of requests that can be enforced night after night, venue after venue, city after city and country after country, is it delicate and essential thing?" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=z2hvUDtBxho
**Concept of the Library** : The idea of collecting all available knowledge in one central place originated many centuries ago, providing a single location for people to find answers to their questions. "thus the concept of the library was born." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**Concert Dynamics: Opening Act Sound** : Opening acts at concerts typically do not sound as loud or "big" as the headliner. This is often because they are not given full use of the PA system, a deliberate decision to prevent them from overshadowing or "blowing off the stage" the main act. "In most cases, they don't sound as big as the headliner. And that's usually because they don't get full use of the PA." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_YjwRApoJhA
**Concert Dynamics: Singer Vocal Adaptations** : During live performances, a main act's singing may differ from studio recordings due to the physical demands on the human voice. Singers often adapt their performance by avoiding high, powerful notes night after night to prevent vocal strain, injury, or cancelling tours. "mostly it's the physical frailties of the human voice. It's hard to hit high, powerful notes night after night." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_YjwRApoJhA
**Concert Dynamics: Volume Ramp-up** : It is a common practice for sound engineers to gradually increase the volume of background music played through the PA system between an opening act and the headliner. This "old sound man trick" is designed to build energy and excitement in the venue before the main performer takes the stage. "the closer it comes to the main event, the louder the music gets. This is an old sound man trick designed to build the energy in the building before the headliner hits the stage." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_YjwRApoJhA
**Concert Nostalgia Boom and Legacy Acts** : The resurgence of interest in "heritage acts" or "legacy bands" from the 1960s, 70s, and 80s has led to a "Concert Nostalgia Boom." These artists now tour in front of very large audiences, with fans willing to pay high prices to see them. This phenomenon is partly driven by the recorded music industry's failure to cultivate a comparable number of new arena- and stadium-filling acts, forcing promoters like Live Nation and AEG to rely on older acts who appeal to an older audience with more disposable income. "Promoters like Live Nation and AEG have had to turn to these older acts who appeal largely to older audiences. We have more disposable income and who are more than willing to relieve their youth one more time." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZzIqIydZTlA
**Concert Rider Function and Importance** : The concert rider is a crucial part of the contract with a promoter, specifying what an act must be provided with before, during, and after a show. Its purpose is to ensure artists are in a "good headspace" to play a great show, covering both mundane logistical details and specific artist requirements. "This brings me to the concert rider, the part of the contract with the promoter that specifies what an act has to be provided with before, during, and after a show." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=z2hvUDtBxho
**Concert Ticket Price Influences: Competition Among Promoters** : Counterintuitively, competition among promoters for popular acts drives concert ticket prices up. Promoters bid against each other by offering increasingly larger guaranteed payments to artists, which are minimum amounts paid regardless of ticket sales. The winning promoter then recoups these higher guarantees and other costs by raising ticket prices, which ultimately trickle down to the fans. "When a band is ready to go on tour, their agent lets promoters in various cities and territories know if there is more than one promoter in a certain city or territory, those who want the show will bid on it." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Concert Ticket Price Influences: Fair Market Value Realization** : Artists and managers have belatedly recognized that concert tickets have historically been underpriced compared to other entertainment events like sports and theater. They are now actively seeking to price tickets at their "fair market value," reflecting the true worth of the performance and demand. "Artists and managers have finally realized that they've been underpricing their concert tickets for years relative to other events like sports and theater." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Concert Ticket Price Influences: Industry Pricing Power** : A third reason for rising concert ticket prices is the realization by artists, managers, promoters, and venues that audiences were willing to pay higher prices for shows. This understanding led to a concerted effort within the industry to increase ticket costs, moving towards "fair market value." "And third, prices went up because the people in the industry, artists, managers, promoters, venues, knew that they could raise prices and we would still pay them." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Concert Ticket Price Influences: Inflation and Touring Economics** : Concert ticket prices have significantly increased over the last three decades due to general inflation and a fundamental shift in touring economics. Historically, tours promoted albums, with record sales covering costs. Today, new records often serve as a pretext for tours, as most artist revenue now comes from live performances. "First, inflation, everything costs more. It's just the way things work. Second, the economics of touring have changed." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Concert Ticket Price Influences: Merchandise Sales Markup** : Bands set profit targets for merchandise sales, and because venues typically take a significant cut (20-50%) of merch sales, artists often compensate by charging higher prices for t-shirts and other merchandise. This strategy contributes to the overall cost for fans attending concerts. "Merch sales go mostly to the band. Although the venue will take a cut of sales, maybe 20, 25%, I've heard as high as 50% in some cases." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Concert Ticketing "Holdbacks"** : A significant portion of concert tickets are held back from the general public sale for various purposes: fan clubs (1,000 tickets in a 15,000 capacity venue example), VIP seating (1,000 tickets), credit card VIP programs (2,000 tickets), artist's use for friends/family (100 tickets), venue promotional use (100 tickets), radio station contests (400 tickets), record label use (100 tickets), and seat licenses (500 tickets for season ticket holders). This can reduce the publicly available pool dramatically. "The first category is called holdbacks. These are tickets reserved for specific purposes." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Concerts that Changed the World (Beyond Obvious Choices)** : While high-profile events like Woodstock, Altamont, and Live Aid have had lasting implications, the focus is on "below the radar gigs" that had subtle, long-term effects. These less obvious concerts are seen as having musical impacts equivalent to small historical triggers. "But are these the only gigs that changed the world? Absolutely not. They're just the most obvious, the ones that most people know." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=huAvyInzz3Q
**Confidential Settlements in Music Disputes** : Many sonic similarity disputes between artists are resolved "behind closed doors" through confidential settlements. This "sort of thing happens all the time" to "keep the piece," with the public typically not being privy to the details. "All teams on both sides are the equation are mobilized. Discussions are held, usually very calmly. And in the end, everything is settled behind closed doors with the public not seeing anything or being privy to anything." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rfObVld9rIE
**Connections TV Show (1970s)** : The BBC premiered a show called 'Connections' in the 1970s, starring science historian James Burke. Over about 20 years and 70 shows, Burke demonstrated how seemingly unconnected scientific achievements, discoveries, and historical events were actually deeply interconnected, forming the basis of modern society, technology, and medicine. "Burke showed how seemingly unconnected scientific achievements and discoveries made it with historical events. And it turns out that they were actually connected, but not in ways you'd have ever expected." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_Pi7JLwAeOI
**Consensus as a Popularity Metric** : Evaluating an act's popularity based on its place in the public's consciousness and the concept of "consensus" may be a more appropriate metric than sales in the current music landscape. "Well, maybe a better way to evaluate an act's popularity is its place in the public's consciousness. And this is where we need to examine the concept of consensus." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z7glrAoXtkA
**Consequence of John Frusciante's Drug Addiction** : During his severe heroin and cocaine addiction, John Frusciante experienced "cocaine psychosis," a condition brought on by excessive use of coke and crack binges lasting for days. This mental state manifested as hallucinations, such as seeing "snakes in his eyes," leading him to make bizarre calls to figures like Perry Farrell. "He later learned that the term for this was cocaine psychosis brought on by way too much coke and crack pinches that would last for days." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TESrEzVHLCc
**Consequence of Removing Shoes During Flight** : During the Learjet 60 crash, Travis Barker had, unusually, taken off his shoes when he took his seat. This seemingly minor detail had severe consequences: when he became covered in jet fuel and caught fire, his fuel-soaked socks continued to burn on his feet and legs, exacerbating his injuries significantly. "This one time, this one time, he took a shoes off when he took a seat. And that meant that fuel soaked his socks and they just kept burning and burning." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Q-QP7ePxkQE
**Constraints on Pleasing Music** : Despite a vast number of potential note combinations, "you just can't stick a bunch of tones and semi-tones together and expect them to sound good." Music needs to be "pleasing to the ear and has to reach into the soul," which significantly narrows down the number of musically viable combinations, especially within specific genres like rock. "Music has to sound pleasing to the ear and has to reach into the soul, right?" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rfObVld9rIE
**Contemporary Record Store Experience** : Despite changes in the music industry, record stores remain valuable spaces for browsing, shopping, and cultural engagement. Visitors can learn by exploring the racks, observing other patrons, and conversing with staff, who often work for the love of music rather than profit. "Although record stores aren't what they used to be, there are still fantastic places to hang out, browse, and shop." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jD1dWxNLvfE
**Continued Presence of Heritage Acts** : A significant factor explaining why 21st-century acts struggle to reach the same heights as older ones is the continued activity of first and second-generation rock bands (e.g., The Stones, Pink Floyd members, U2, Eagles, The Who, Dylan, Paul McCartney) who still make records and tour, competing for audience attention and money. "Another issue that could explain why 21st century acts haven't been able to scale the same height as older ones is because those old acts are still with us." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z7glrAoXtkA
**Contradictory Word Pairs** : Several common word pairs are noted for their inherent contradiction despite frequent usage, including "Alone together," "Defining silence," "Definitely maybe," "Random order," "Walking Dead," and "Original copy." "Here's a list of words that should not go together, but do. Alone together. Defining silence. Definitely maybe. Random order, walking dead, original copy." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=pE7TaWSUa-o
**Contrast of North American New Wave vs. British Post-Punk** : In North America, new wave was a commercial success, marketed as a milder form of punk, enriching labels and artists. In Britain, however, punk's initial intensity burnt out quickly by 1979, giving way to a more complex, experimental, and moodier sound called post-punk, reflecting the country's severe economic and social hardships. "In North America, these bands and these sounds were marketed as new wave, the less angry, less violent, less political, and more well-behaved cousin of punk rock. And it worked very well too. Lots of bands became famous and record labels got rich. However, things were different on the other side of the Atlantic." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=9uTORLOm5Yg
**Controversial Music Choice for Donald Trump's Campaign** : Donald Trump's presidential campaign was criticized for its music selections, notably playing Creedence Clearwater Revival's "Fortunate Son." This song was originally inspired by the issue of wealthy individuals receiving draft deferments during the Vietnam War, a situation that mirrored Trump's own five draft deferments. The use of the song was further controversial as it occurred simultaneously with reports that Trump had referred to soldiers and war dead as "losers and suckers." "Whoever chose the music for his presidential campaign was awful. At one stop, they played fortune at sun by credence clearwater revival, which was inspired by the kids of rich and connected parents getting draft affirmants for Vietnam, which is exactly what happened with Donald Trump five times." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HoBx6s3JmVQ
**Copyright Infringement Trials - "Note by Note" Comparison** : In copyright infringement trials, the judge and jury do not simply listen to recordings. Instead, "both sides get deep into music theory with their arguments," and the decision relies solely on "the notes that make up the song," as plotted on sheet music. "The only thing that matters are just the notes, how the song looks when it's plotted out on sheet music." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rfObVld9rIE
**Copyright Infringement Trials - Production vs. Notes** : During copyright trials, while recordings "may sound the same," this introduces "other elements like production techniques and outboard effects," which change the "nature of the song." However, these elements are disregarded, and "the only thing that matters are just the notes." "Now, the recording may sound the same, but that introduces other elements like production techniques and outboard effects, which changes the nature of the song." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rfObVld9rIE
**Copyright and Master Ownership in Licensing** : An artist stands to earn "big dollars" from a licensing deal if they own both the copyright to the song (publishing rights) and the master recording. However, these rights are commonly shared with a music publisher and a record company, respectively. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sfie4YYncxk
**Cordiospinal Excitability (Urge to Dance)** : A song featuring a compelling beat or groove can promote something known as "cordiospinal excitability," which is essentially the urge to dance. This physiological response involves pumping extra blood to the legs and changes to heart rate and breathing patterns, allowing the body to instinctively sync with the music. "if a song has a good beat or a good groove, it promotes something called cordiospinal excitability, otherwise known as the urge to dance." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mTbagQE7akQ
**Core Business of Record Stores** : A record store's fundamental business model involves securing physical space, amassing inventory, employing staff, and undertaking all necessary activities to sell recorded music on behalf of record labels. This venture inherently carries substantial risk, as predicting sales success beyond major superstar releases is difficult. "It acquires space, a masses inventory, hires people, and does whatever it has to do to sell recorded music on behalf of record labels." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jD1dWxNLvfE
**Coronavirus Impact on Moshing** : The coronavirus pandemic introduced a new, invisible deterrent to moshing. The idea of close physical contact with sweaty strangers and exposure to bodily fluids made the activity unappealing, potentially marking a temporary or permanent end to moshing until widespread immunity is achieved. "The idea of slamming up against sweaty strangers and all their bodily fluids and spit isn't the kind of thing that sounds very good anymore." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=KMx4bmq8o2Y
**Correct Plural of "Vinyl"** : The correct plural form of the word "vinyl" is "vinyl," akin to "deer." Referring to multiple records as "vinyls" is considered incorrect and a tell-tale sign of an inexperienced collector or "newbie." "The plural of vinyl is like the plural of deer. You have a single deer named Bambi or whatever, and you also had a herd of deer, not deers. You also have a piece of vinyl, a single record, and also a collection of vinyl." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B2g4QSREzpw
**Cosmology Analogy to Punk Rock** : The Big Bang theory, describing the universe's origin from an infinitely dense singularity 16 billion years ago, is used as an analogy for punk rock in the mid-1970s. Punk is seen as a "musical big bang" whose ideas and attitudes spread out and coalesced into new musical "life forms." "Sometime around 16 billion years ago, there was this infinitely dense and infinitely tiny thing called a singularity... If we look at the punk rock of the middle 1970s, we can think of this as a musical big bang." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=9uTORLOm5Yg
**Cost and Time Investment of Tattoos** : Tattoos are not cheap; Goldfinger drummer Darren Fyfer estimated spending between $30,000 to $50,000 on his own tattoos. The time spent in the tattoo chair can also be extensive, with Fyfer estimating a cumulative week of tattooing, ranging from one to eight hours per session depending on size and complexity. "They're not cheap. No, they're not. Well over, maybe 50 grand. You're kidding. All over, yeah. Maybe 30, 35. Okay. And how long do you think you spent in a chair? Another great question. I don't know how to answer it. If I had to venture a guess, I would say probably a week." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=jZeMmPekKMQ

**Crapulence** : A real word that describes the sick feeling experienced after eating or drinking too much. "And the word, crapulence, is real. It's that sick feeling that you get when you eat or drink too much." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_YjwRApoJhA
**Creative Commons Attribution Non-Commercial Share-A-Like License** : This license was used by Trent Reznor for Nine Inch Nails' instrumental album "Ghosts I–IV," released in 2008. It allowed anyone to use the album's content for non-profit purposes, encouraging other composers. However, when a Dutch beatmaker remixed "34 Ghosts 4" and sold it, and Lil Nas X then used it for the commercially successful "Old Town Road," it constituted a violation. This led to Trent Reznor and Atticus Ross receiving official songwriting credits and sharing in "Old Town Road"'s chart records, including its 19 weeks at number one on the Billboard Hot 100. "And in an interesting twist, Ghosts 1-4 was released under a Creative Commons Attribution Non-Commercial Share-A-Like License. This meant that anyone could use anything on the album for anything at all as long as it was not for profit." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DKdF8NgF5Bw
**Creativity Versus Schedule** : The nature of creative work, particularly in music, defies rigid scheduling; true creativity and inspiration are spontaneous, contrasting sharply with the predictable, assembly-line production model of manufacturing. "You cannot be brilliant on a schedule. Creativity and inspiration happens when it happens." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vt6DVj7S6nk

**Cup Holders in Venues for Increased Sales** : The widespread presence of cup holders in amphitheaters, arenas, and stadiums serves a purpose beyond convenience: they are primarily designed to enable attendees to purchase and hold two drinks simultaneously. This encourages greater alcohol sales, as fans can store a second beer while consuming their first. **Cynthia Plaster Caster – Rock Star Penis Mold Collection Since 1968, Kiss Song, Documentary** : Chicago-born Cynthia Albritten (known professionally as Cynthia Plaster Caster) developed her unique hobby after a high school art class assignment in plaster casting around 1968. Her specialty is making plaster molds of the penises of willing rock musicians; confirmed subjects include Jimi Hendrix and Jello Biafra of the Dead Kennedys, among many others. She also casts willing women's breasts. She maintains records of those who attempted but were not "up to the task." Kiss wrote "Plaster Caster" about her on their *Love Gun* album. A documentary film about her was made, and she continues to exhibit her work in art galleries. "Her thing was getting plaster casts of rock star penises." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=lyYsTon_NSc
**D22 Club - Beijing Punk Venue** : D22 was a specific punk club located on the "outskirts of Beijing, China," where legendary record executive Seymour Stein went to listen to local Chinese punk and alternative bands around 1 AM, demonstrating his interest in emerging global music scenes. "We're going up to this punk club called D22 to see some of the local talent." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hjProJd_JrM
**DAB Digital Audio Broadcasting – Norway 99.7%, Canada and US 0%** : Digital Audio Broadcasting is an entirely separate set of frequencies broadcasting MP2 digital signals, developed in Europe as a replacement for analog AM and FM. While Norway has achieved 99.7% population coverage, Switzerland 99.5%, and Germany 98%, the United States refused to adopt the European standard (citing interference with military frequencies), meaning Canada and the US have zero DAB coverage. European countries satisfied with DAB have begun shutting down their AM and FM transmitters entirely, leaving North America technologically behind. As a compromise, the US developed "HD Radio" — a trademark (like Kleenex) for digital signals piggybacked on existing FM broadcasts, standard in cars since around 2012 but attracting negligible public awareness. "Ask a person on the street about HD radio and they'll go, what?" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=iyGUXdDqIlI
**DARPA's Social Media Analysis of Pop Stars (2014)** : In 2014, the Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency (DARPA), based within the Pentagon, received funding to analyze tweets from Lady Gaga and Justin Bieber. The agency was specifically looking for linguistic cues, patterns of information flow, and the detection of sentiment or opinion within information spread through social media, suspecting that some tweets might be fake and used to influence public opinion. "In 2014 DARPA, the defense advanced research projects agency, which is based inside the Pentagon, was funded to analyze tweets sent out by Lady Gaga and Justin Bieber." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GO0K_N7Ys7I
**DIY Ethic as Inspiration** : The radical act of two individuals in Manchester starting their own record label, as seen with the Buzzcocks and New Hormones, served as a massive inspiration for countless others determined to get underground music heard. In the four years following this event, over 900 independent records were released in Britain using a similar self-release model. "Well, I went through the South Korean side and went on the South Korean DMZ side and there's these huts in the middle where they do all the negotiations and there's a conference table with a line down the middle that represents the border between North and South Korea. **De-rockification of Major Music Festivals** : Major music festivals like Lollapalooza and Coachella have significantly evolved away from their original rock roots, particularly starting around 2008. Lollapalooza, initially a full-blown touring festival of alternative and rock bands in the 1990s, is now primarily a pop and hip-hop event. Similarly, Coachella, which began in 1999 with a focus on rock, shifted towards pop and hip-hop headliners due to a cultural shift in America, where these genres became dominant among the festival-going demographic. While both festivals still include token rock appearances, their primary lineup strategies cater to youth-driven preferences for pop and hip-hop to ensure ticket sales. "The evolution of two major major music festivals away from their rock roots. When La La Palosa was first conceived in 1990, it was a full-blown touring festival of Weirdos, featuring the best in left-of-center and alternative bands." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZzIqIydZTlA
**Deals with Ticket Brokers/Scalpers** : Promoters, ticket sellers, and venues often have existing deals with ticket brokers, sometimes referred to as scalpers, who resell tickets on the secondary market. This practice allows the primary sellers to unload tickets, transferring financial risk to the secondary market, and records those tickets as sold, even though they haven't reached the end consumer yet. "The promoter, the ticket seller, and the venue may have existing deals with ticket brokers, companies that resell tickets on the secondary market." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE

**Death of Album Art – CD Shrank Canvas from 12-Inch to 5-Inch; Now Digital Means No Physical Canvas at All** : The 12x12-inch canvas of the LP shrank to the 5x5-inch booklet of the CD, though CDs allowed multi-page booklets to compensate. Now, as music goes fully digital and album sales continue to decline, the physical canvas for album art is essentially gone. Hundreds of billions of dollars that once went to music now go to DVDs, video games, streaming TV, smartphones, and other entertainment, leading to a dramatically declining need for album cover art. "We could be witnessing the death of an art that extends back nearly a century. And that would be sad." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JdSUHkXM1mQ
