**The KLF's Pyramid of Human Ashes** : The KLF, a renowned music and art collective, is undertaking a project in Liverpool to build a pyramid from the ashes of 34,592 deceased individuals. Participants can have their cremated remains transformed into a brick for inclusion in the pyramid, with a reservation fee of £99. "When you die, you get cremated and have your ashes formed into a brick. That brick will be added to this pyramid being built in Liverpool." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=HoBx6s3JmVQ
**The Killers Sam's Town Cover (2006) – Anton Corbijn; Dead Stuffed Longhorn Sheep; Damaged Beauty Queen Production Assistant** : The Killers hired Anton Corbijn again for Sam's Town. Corbijn, put in a similar desert headspace as with U2, wanted a dead sheep for the shoot — the Longhorn is the official sheep of Nevada, and a live one would be too camera-shy and vain. He rented a dead, stuffed Longhorn from the local museum. Corbijn also wanted someone who looked like a "damaged beauty queen" and found production assistant Felice LaZay at the Las Vegas recording studio where the Killers recorded the album. He took Felice and the dead stuffed sheep to an abandoned mobile home in the blazing desert and photographed them. The best shot of the dozens taken became the album cover. "So he rented one, a dead one, a stuffed one from the local museum." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JdSUHkXM1mQ
**The Killers – Named After a Fictional Band in a New Order Music Video** : The name came from the name of a fictional band appearing on the bass drum in the video for New Order's "Crystal." "That was the name of a fictional band in the video." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NCQ1ERgSfeE
**The Loudness Wars (Early 2000s Peak)** : The "loudness wars" was a regrettable trend, peaking in the early 2000s, where songs were mastered with excessive compression to make them sound louder and "pop more" on radios and speakers. This technological race in the digital era flattened the dynamic range of original recordings, boosting quiet parts to be as loud as the loudest parts, creating an illusion of volume but resulting in distortion, lack of clarity, and significant loss of detail, leading to listener fatigue. Fortunately, pushback from organizations like Dynamic Range Day and streaming platforms (Apple Music, Spotify, YouTube) normalizing playback levels has reduced the incentive for loud mastering, though some "audio atrocities" like CD editions of Californication and Metallica's St. Anger remain. "The loudness wars refers to mastering songs with tons of compression, making them sound louder than they actually are." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZzIqIydZTlA
**The Loudness Wars Explained** : The "Loudness Wars" refer to a trend where artists, producers, and record labels aim to make their music sound louder by applying more compression to recordings, squishing the natural dynamic range of songs. This practice is driven by the belief that louder music stands out more, but it often leads to a lack of dynamic range, distortion even at low volumes, and listener fatigue. Key concepts include 'level' (technical sound pressure), 'volume' (subjective device-related setting), and 'loudness' (perceived difference between soft and loud parts, which increases with compression but not necessarily with level or volume). "artists, producers and record labels have gotten to this arms war over loudness. They believe that if something sounds louder it will stand out." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Yl2poVWguu0
**The Loudness Wars Phenomenon** : Over the past two decades, the music industry has seen "the loudness wars," a trend driven by record labels, producers, managers, and artists insisting on maximizing the intrinsic loudness of recordings during the mastering process. This is achieved through aggressive audio compression, which "squeezes in as much musical data as you can," resulting in "really squished music" with significantly reduced dynamic range, making quiet passages sound as loud or louder than intended. Modern CDs can be up to "20 decibels louder than they were back in the 80s and 90s," which flattens subtleties, increases perceived distortion, and leads to "listener fatigue." This practice is based on the unproven belief that louder recordings stand out more and are more successful, and has been exacerbated by the rise of MP3s and low-quality earbuds. "The trend has become that to make the default loudness, the default force by which the music seems to come out of the speakers and headphones, to be as great as possible." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7Zniri9uSlc
**The MAPL System – Defining What Counts as Canadian Content** : Stan Klees developed the MAPL system (after a pie chart with four quadrants printed on Canadian releases): M = Music composed by a Canadian; A = Artist performing is principally Canadian; P = Production in a Canadian studio (or live Canadian broadcast); L = Lyrics written by a Canadian. A song qualifies as CanCon if it meets at least two of these four criteria. This creates surprising outcomes: Lenny Kravitz's "American Woman" qualifies because music and lyrics were written by Canadians (Burton Cummings and Randy Bachman), even though Kravitz was born in New York. Bryan Adams's "Everything I Do (I Do It for You)" did NOT qualify because collaborators Mutt Lange (British) and Michael Kamen (American) brought it below the two-point threshold — which led to the so-called "Brian Adams Rule" that now allows more leniency for international collaborations. "Still my least favorite record." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-JWO_jYXwEM
**The Magic Shop Studio** : The Magic Shop was a studio located literally steps from David Bowie's New York apartment, where he secretly recorded his 2013 comeback album *The Next Day* with a skeleton staff to maintain absolute secrecy. "No hangers on, no unnecessary people in the building at the magic shop. Name of the studio, which was literally steps from his apartment." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Yq2A_o9-ulM
**The Mansion Recording Studio (351 Laurel Canyon Blvd)** : A historic mansion built in 1918 at 351 Laurel Canyon Boulevard served as the isolated, phone-free recording studio for the Red Hot Chili Peppers' "Blood Sugar Sex Magik" album in April 1991. Reputedly haunted with a history of death and gangster activity, its basement was converted into a studio by a Canadian company under producer Rick Rubin's direction. The house was once believed to be a residence of Harry Houdini, though this is unproven. "In April 1991, the Chili Peppers moved into a historic mansion at 351 Laurel Canyon, Boulevard that was built in 1918." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TESrEzVHLCc
**The Masked Wrestler Theobald Bauer (1865) – New York State Prohibited Wrestling Masks Until 1972** : The first professional wrestler to appear in a mask was Theobald Bauer, a French circus performer who began a masked wrestling career in 1865. The concept eventually took off in Mexico and Japan but was slow to arrive in the United States because a New York state law prohibited professional wrestlers from wearing masks during matches — a law that stayed in effect until 1972. "I have no idea why that law existed in the first place and it stayed in effect until 1972." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=DiyIPBKiVW4
**The Me Too Reckoning in the Music Industry** : The music industry has undergone a slow but significant "Me Too reckoning" since around 2006, addressing decades of unchecked predatory behavior, sexual assault, and misogyny. Historically, the industry's "sex, drugs, and rock and roll" ethos allowed abusers to operate with impunity, shielded by wealth, privilege, and "fixers." The Me Too movement, which gained traction on MySpace around 2006 and spread across social media, empowered women to share their stories and expose abusers. This led to perpetrators being held accountable through legal action, "cancellation" of careers, increased attention to challenges faced by female performers, and efforts to create safer spaces. The proliferation of smartphones and video recording also made bad behavior riskier. While significant progress has been made compared to earlier eras, further work is still needed to dismantle deeply ingrained systemic issues. "It's the slow, me too reckoning within the music industry. For decades, predators, sexual assaults, and other awful people got away with the craziest and most vile things when it came to the treatment of women, stuff that goes far beyond, you know, usual misogyny." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZzIqIydZTlA
**The Muffin Man Nursery Rhyme's Alleged Origin** : The nursery rhyme "The Muffin Man" is alleged to have been inspired by Frederick Thomas Lindwood, a 16th-century baker who became a serial killer. Lindwood reportedly lured 16 children by attaching a muffin to a string and dragging it down the street, and also murdered seven rival pastry chefs. "It's alleged that it was inspired by a 16th century baker named Frederick Thomas Lindwood who turned into a serial killer." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FtGJHhqXcDI
**The New Pornographers – Named After a 1966 Japanese Film; Has Caused Misunderstandings** : Guitarist A.C. Newman caught the name from a 1966 Japanese film called The Pornographers. It was also a nod to bandmate Dan Bejar, who had a band called Destroyer with a track called "The Pornographers." The name has caused many real-world misunderstandings over the years with people assuming the band is involved in pornography. **The Obelisk / Easy Cure (later The Cure) – The Cure's Pre-History** : The Cure were first called The Obelisk, then the Easy Cure, before finally settling on The Cure. "They turned into the easy cure and finally just the cure." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QkYHaJKohVQ
**The Pogo Dance** : The Pogo dance, a precursor to moshing, involves standing in place and jumping up and down. It emerged during the original punk rock era in the UK in the 1970s, partly as a necessity in venues without stages where jumping was the only way to see the band. Sid Vicious is often credited with its invention, either to mock non-punks or due to movement restrictions from his attire. "The most direct ancestor of mashing is the Pogo." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=KMx4bmq8o2Y
**The Police – Stuart Copeland's CIA Father Made "Police" More Subversive** : "Police" was provocative in the punk era. Other names considered included Hypochondria, Grand Larceny, and Assault. "Police" won because drummer Stuart Copeland's father worked for the CIA, making the name feel doubly subversive. Interpol (the band) was subsequently named after the real international police organization headquartered in Lyon, France. "Police worn because drummer Stuart Copeland's father was in the CIA." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QkYHaJKohVQ
**The Power of Shared History and Conversation** : Memories can be unreliable, but engaging in conversation, especially within a group that shares a common history, can trigger a flood of forgotten details and serve as a cathartic, therapeutic, nostalgic, and enjoyable experience. "if you have a group of people with a shared history and they start talking, it's amazing what comes flooding back." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=zBd13KtYlAA
**The Record Store Experience (Pre-Internet Era)** : In the era before widespread internet access, acquiring music involved a significant experience: leaving home, traveling to a record store, and physically browsing shelves. This process offered opportunities for discovery, interaction with new music, special ordering specific titles, and social engagement with friends or store clerks who often provided recommendations. **The The – Deliberately Hardest Band Name to Search Online; Punk Trend of "The" Bands** : Singer Matt Johnson was advised by friend Keith that in the late 1970s, all new punk bands were "The [something]." So the most trendy and punk name would be "The The" — the ultimate example of the genre's naming convention. It was also nearly impossible to search for online when early search engines ignored definite articles. "What could be more trendy in punk than calling your group the the?" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QkYHaJKohVQ
**The Tube (British TV Show)** : This British TV program was the setting for Michael Hutchence and Paula Yates's first meeting in 1985, where their undeniable connection first emerged. "In 1985, Hutchins was a guest on the British TV show The Tube." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=23OgreyK66o
**The White Stripes – Meg White's Love of Red and White Peppermint Candies** : When Jack met Meg, she was obsessed with red and white peppermint candies. **The Worst Song Ever Scientifically Engineered – 20 Minutes; Only 200 People Would Like It** : Three scientists (Vitaly Komar, Alex Melamid, and David Soldier) gathered data on musical elements that people dislike and used AI to engineer the worst song possible. The resulting 20-minute composition features cowboy lyrics, bagpipes, bossa nova synth beats, a children's choir, nonsensical spoken word, a man yelling through a bullhorn, and more. Their calculation: only approximately 200 of 8 billion people on earth would find it pleasurable. "Only about 200 of the 8 billion people on this planet would find their composition pleasurable." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=fnZx8EGnszw
**The Worther Effect (Copycat Suicides)** : The Worther Effect describes a phenomenon where widely publicized suicides, particularly those of well-known musicians, can lead to an increase in "copycat" suicides among fans. Kurt Cobain's death is cited as an example, while Marilyn Monroe's death saw a 12% rise in US suicides, making it the most pronounced example. "When a well-known musician commits suicide, psychologist warn of copycat suicides by fans. This is known as the Worther Effect." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aSk1XQHNkd8
**The Young Aborigines (later Beastie Boys) – Beastie Boys' Original Name** : The Beastie Boys were originally called The Young Aborigines. "The young Aborigines, who became the Beastie Boys." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QkYHaJKohVQ
**Thomas Edison's Phonograph** : Thomas Edison devised the phonograph, an early sound storage machine that used a cylinder coated with hard wax to record sound in grooves. "While Thomas Edison figured out how to store sound in grooves, he used a cylinder coated with a hard wax and his machine was called the phonograph." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=IIJySSo276k
**Thomas Edison's Phonograph (1877) – Not Originally Conceived for Music** : Thomas Edison's phonograph, patented in 1877, was not initially conceived as a device for music reproduction. Edison listed music very low on his enumerated potential uses, prioritizing speech preservation, dictation, and remote learning. "Edison listed music reproduction very low on his list of potential uses." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=OrHtPVBjXw4
**Thomas Wilhelm's Murder of Girlfriend (Year not given)** : Thomas Wilhelm fatally shot his live-in girlfriend after listening to Guns N' Roses' "Used to Love Her (but I Had to Kill Her)" on repeat while he was drunk. This incident highlights the song's tragic association with real-world violence. "That same song resulted in the death of the live-in girlfriend of Thomas Wilhelm. He was drunk and listened to the song on repeat before he shot her." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=FtGJHhqXcDI
**Thrasher Magazine** : This magazine played a crucial role in connecting skaters with the associated bands, covering both skateboarding and the new breed of punk acts from regions like Oxnard, Orange County, and San Francisco. **Three Types of Bands** : There are three types of bands: the extreme (doing anything for attention), the traditional (earnest and honest), and the quirky, eccentric, and weird. The third type, which Weezer falls into, makes music fun and unpredictable, balancing uniqueness with widespread appeal. "The way I see it, there are three types of bands that stretch across a spectrum." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LYRPxtP61JM
**Three Types of Bootleg Recordings – Audience, Soundboard, and Studio Outtakes** : There are three main categories of bootleg recordings: audience recordings (variable quality, with ambient crowd noise), soundboard recordings (professionally mixed, clean audio), and studio outtakes (demos, alternate takes, and rehearsals — the most coveted type for their insight into the creative process). "The three types are: audience recordings, soundboard recordings, and studio takes (demos, outtakes, rehearsals — the most valued)." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Uly2cT2vc28
**Tiatrophone Expansion and Decline** : The Tiatrophone, or Electrophone in the UK, saw services roll out across Europe and was even demonstrated in New York. It operated as a subscription service for live music until 1931, when it was ultimately rendered obsolete by the advent of radio, which offered "wireless" transmission. "The Tiatrophone services rolled out across Europe. It appeared in the UK under the name Electrophone. And the technology was even demonstrated in New York." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VnXBJqKwkrw
**Ticketmaster Acquisitions (1998-2000)** : Ticketmaster expanded its market presence through strategic acquisitions. In 1998, it absorbed City Search, and in 2000, it purchased Ticket Web, further consolidating its position as the internet became increasingly prevalent for ticket sales. "Ticket master absorbed a company called City Search in 1998. Another ticketing company called Ticket Web was bought in 2000." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Ticketmaster Website Launch (June 1995)** : Ticketmaster launched its first website in June 1995, initially serving as a searchable database of events without direct purchasing capabilities. Users still had to call a phone line or visit a physical outlet to buy tickets. "Ticketmaster's first website was launched in June 1995." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Ticketmaster's Emergence and Strategy (Early 1990s)** : Ticketmaster emerged as the dominant computerized ticket seller by 1991, outlasting rivals like Ticketron. Its success stemmed from its approach as a ticket service utilizing computers, rather than a computer company dabbling in tickets, and its reputation for a "rock-solid backend" in technical operations. "We finished the last episode in 1991 with ticket buster emerging as the dominant seller of concert tickets." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Ticketmaster's Free Hardware/Software Model** : Ticketmaster provided its clients (venues, promoters) with ticketing software and hardware for free. This model was financially sustainable through the imposition of service charges on every ticket sold, which covered Ticketmaster's costs and generated profit without taking a cut of the ticket's face value. "Like some of its predecessors, Ticketmaster gave its clients the software and hardware for free." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Ticketmaster's Vertical Integration Strategy (2008-2009)** : Ticketmaster pursued vertical integration within the concert industry, aiming to own key elements of the music business to become stronger. This strategy led to the acquisition of artist management company Frontline Management in 2008 and the major merger with Live Nation, the biggest concert promoter, in February 2009, forming Live Nation Entertainment. "Plus, ticket master began to see the possibilities of vertical integration within the concert industry." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Ticketron's Role in Ticketing History** : Ticketron was a predecessor in the computerized ticketing space that eventually became defunct and was later absorbed by Ticketmaster. It was characterized as a "computer company that sold tickets," contrasting with Ticketmaster's identity as a "ticket service that made use of computers." "Ticketmaster was the former, the defunct Ticketron was the latter." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Tiered and VIP Pricing** : Concert ticket pricing has evolved to include tiered pricing, where seats closer to the stage cost more, and VIP sections that offer additional benefits like soundcheck access or meet-and-greets for even higher prices. This allows artists to maximize revenue from higher-demand seating and unique fan experiences. "First, there was tiered pricing. Better seats closer to the stage cost more. Believe it or not, that wasn't always the case." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**Time to Play All Unique Combinations** : If one were to play a new combination of notes every second, it would take an astounding 33 billion, 63 million, 236,360 years to exhaust all one quintillion non-repeating sequences using the 12 notes of the chromatic scale. This is approximately two and a half times the age of the universe, demonstrating the immense, though finite, potential of musical expression. "if you played a new combination of notes every second, it would take you 33 billion, 63 million, 236,360 years, which is about two and a half times the age of the universe." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=L09R2evjyMc
**Times Square Hotel - New York Dolls' First Gig** : The New York Dolls played their very first gig at a "sleazy hotel in Times Square" in the spring of 1972. This venue served as the initial stage for their shocking and provocative image, complete with makeup, spandex, and fishnets, as they introduced their unique take on glam rock. "Their first gig was at a sleazy hotel in Times Square." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0xd0Hf5REg4
**Tina Bell's Posthumous Recognition Efforts** : At the time of Tina Bell's death in 2012, efforts began to recognize her legacy, including a documentary and memoir titled "Conversations with the Grunge Queen." Her son, TJ, an Academy Award-winning documentary co-producer, is actively involved in these efforts to ensure her contributions are acknowledged. "At the time of Tina's death in 2012, there was an effort to finally recognize her legacy with a documentary in memoir called Conversations with the Grunge Queen." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VnXBJqKwkrw
**Tina Bell's Racial and Gender Barriers** : One theory for Tina Bell and Bam Bam's lack of recognition in grunge history is that audiences and critics might have been unable to accept a black woman fronting a "punky rock band in a man's world." This suggests that race and gender played a role in their being overlooked. "I mean, a black woman, fronting this kind of punky rock band in a man's world. Were audiences and critics unable to grasp such a person doing such a thing? Possibly." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VnXBJqKwkrw
**Tina Turner Music for Bird Control at Airport** : An airport situated in Gloucestershire, UK, successfully resolved an issue with birds on its runway by implementing an unconventional solution. They continuously blasted music from Tina Turner, twenty-four hours a day, effectively deterring the birds from the area. "With an airport and Gloucestershire in the UK had a problem with birds on the runway, they solved that by blasting them with music from Tina Turner, twenty four hours a day." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GO0K_N7Ys7I

**Top 40 Radio – Invented in Omaha by Todd Storz After Observing Jukebox Repetition** : The origin of top 40 radio traces to Todd Storz and Bill Stewart's efforts to rescue a failing Omaha AM station called KOWH. One story holds that around 1951 they observed bar patrons repeatedly playing the same songs all night on a jukebox, inspiring the idea of doing the same on radio. A more documented version: Storz ran a New Orleans station and, competing against a rival show called "The Top 20 at 1280," launched "The Top 40 at 1450" one hour earlier. When he moved to Omaha, he expanded the concept to 24 hours. KOWH went from a 4% to a 45% market share within six months. The format spread worldwide and dominates commercial radio to this day. "You can thank or blame Todd Storz for top 40 radio." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=iyGUXdDqIlI

"Back in the day when music was harder to come by, a record label could afford to wait for a band to develop and mature through 2, 3, 4, 5 albums." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ooNAHswiT5Q
**Traditional Record Deal Duration** : Historically, a first record deal typically spans around seven albums, a structure designed to provide both security for the artist, allowing them time to develop and mature their sound over several releases, and an opportunity for the record label to see a return on their significant upfront investment. "Traditionally, when you sign your first record deal, it's usually for around seven albums." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=vt6DVj7S6nk

**Transistor Radio (1954) – Portable Device That Made the Teenage Cultural Revolution Possible** : Until 1954, radios were large pieces of furniture that provided communal family listening experiences. The transistor radio changed everything: pocket-sized, portable, and — crucially — equipped with an earphone that allowed private listening. It arrived at precisely the same moment as rock and roll and the newly constructed social category of the teenager. Young people could take their forbidden music anywhere, out of sight and out of earshot of parents. "The transistor radio and rock and roll arrived at exactly the same time. This new social and economic construct called a teenager could then take his or her music with them everywhere." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=iyGUXdDqIlI

**Two Types of Live Bootleg Recordings – Audience vs. Soundboard** : There are two primary categories of live bootleg recordings: audience recordings (captured with concealed microphones, raw and ambient) and soundboard recordings (tapped directly from the venue's PA system, offering clean, professional-quality audio). "There are mainly two categories: audience recordings (raw, with public atmosphere) and soundboard recordings (connected directly to the PA, offering clean audio)." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=OrHtPVBjXw4
**U2 The Joshua Tree Cover (1986) – Anton Corbijn; 20-Minute Rest Stop in Mojave Desert; Joshua Tree Died and Fell ~2000** : While finishing The Joshua Tree, U2 hired rock photographer Anton Corbijn and flew to Los Angeles in December 1986 to shoot in the Mojave Desert near Death Valley, driving around in a hired bus and staying in crappy motels. When someone asked the bus driver about the strange cactus-like plants, he said they were called Joshua trees — Bono thought it was a great name for a tree and they stopped for 20 minutes to take photos. When examining the contact sheets, those 20-minute rest-stop shots were judged the best, somehow capturing and encapsulating everything the band was about. U2 never revealed the specific tree's location to prevent vandalism. Despite precautions, the tree died and fell over sometime in fall 2000, about 200 years old. Intrepid fans have since found its remains; some leave a ring of stones in the shape of a heart. "When it came time to examine the contact sheets of all the photos that have been taken, the shots from that 20 minute rest stop were judged to be the best." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JdSUHkXM1mQ
**U2 – Name Suggested by Steve Averill of Radiators from Space; Chosen from a List of Ten** : When Adam Clayton called Dublin ad agency worker Steve Averill asking for a band rename (from The Hype), Averill suggested a list including U2 because abbreviations were fashionable (XTC, etc.). The band chose U2. Averill is now U2's art director. "Steve is now working as U2's art director." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NCQ1ERgSfeE
**UK Musicians Union vs. Synthesizers** : On May 23, 1982, the UK Musicians Union publicly expressed grievances over the increasing use of synthesizers, which they believed threatened the jobs of "real musicians." This culminated in a motion passing at a union meeting that called for a ban on all synthesizers, a stance that was met with ridicule from the music press and fans. "On May 23, 1982, which just happened to be the 48th birthday of Synth Pioneer Bob Mogue, the UK Musicians Union went public with their grievances." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=5PJRCC6EwUY
**UN Treaty Threatening Podcasting (Past Decade)** : At one point in the past decade, a proposed UN treaty from the World Intellectual Property Rights Association aimed to prohibit the aggregation and mirroring of online content, regardless of its copyright status. Had it passed, this treaty could have rendered podcasting illegal. "The UN's World Intellectual Property Rights Association wanted to prohibit the ability to aggregate and mirror content online, whether it's copyrighted or not." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aSk1XQHNkd8
**US Historical Censorship of Music – Jazz Banned on Sundays (1920s), R&B Called "Dirty Music" (1930s–50s)** : Despite the First Amendment, the United States has a long history of attempting to censor music. In the 1920s, there were efforts to ban jazz on Sundays on the grounds that it was "evil." In the 1930s through 1950s, rhythm and blues — music primarily by Black artists — was routinely described as "dirty music." "In the 1920s there were attempts to ban jazz on Sundays as 'evil,' and in the 30s–50s R&B was called 'dirty music.'" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=f4TOWLYRnGs

**US Justice Department's Antitrust Scrutiny (1991-1995)** : The US Justice Department began monitoring Ticketmaster following its 1991 merger with Ticketron. Their interest intensified with Pearl Jam's public disputes over service charges, leading to an encouraged antitrust complaint in 1995, though the investigation ultimately closed without significant government action, with a promise to continue monitoring the industry. "They'd been keeping tabs on ticket master since the 1991 merger with Ticketron." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8q2bRZyHnHE
**US Radio Airplay Royalties for Cover Songs** : In the United States, artists performing cover songs on American radio do not receive airplay royalties. These royalties are exclusively directed to the original composers and publishers of the song, meaning bands like Hootie & the Blowfish only benefited from mechanical royalties (sales) for their covers. "if you are an American artist on American radio and you're playing a cover, you don't reap any radio airplay royalties from that because it only goes to the composers." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=W9gdKha0PLg
**Ubiquity of Cell Phones at Concerts (Post-2007)** : Before the advent of smartphones around 2007, concert tickets often carried warnings against cameras and recording devices, reflecting a strict industry stance against bootlegging and unauthorized photography. However, with the rise of smartphones, enforcing these rules became a "losing battle," leading promoters and artists to generally accept or adapt to their presence. Reactions vary from making concerts "Instagramable and TikTok Ready" (e.g., Coldplay) to strict crackdowns by artists who demand fans be "in the moment" (e.g., Green Day) or even requiring phones to be locked in bags (e.g., Jack White). This shift has also created a "generational divide" between younger fans who integrate phones into the concert experience and older attendees who find it disruptive, becoming part of a larger conversation about technology in public spaces. "But with the rise of smartphones after about 2007, this became a losing battle." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=xid8CZf4oYo
**Ugandan Radio Stations Shut Down for Promoting Witchcraft** : Only Radio Stations, with its twenty-three outlets in Uganda, faced government-mandated shutdown. **Unfortunate Sonic Coincidences** : These refer to situations where two distinct songs end up sounding remarkably similar despite what should be extremely astronomical odds, suggesting independent creation rather than deliberate copying. The internet's reach has made people more aware of such coincidences. **Union of Sound Synthesists Formation** : In direct response to the UK Musicians Union's call for a ban on synthesizers in May 1982, an opposition party named the Union of Sound Synthesists was formed. This group countered the perceived absurdity of limiting technological progress, standing up for electronic music and its practitioners. "The whole situation did not go down well. It prompted the formation of an opposition party called the Union of Sound Synthesists, and the Music Press and Music Fans called this idea the product of a bunch of loonies." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=5PJRCC6EwUY
**Universal Decline in Record Sales** : No artist, including established "Heritage Acts" like Pearl Jam, Green Day, Nine Inch Nails, and Metallica, is currently selling records in the same numbers as they did in the past, and such high album sales (e.g., 20 million copies) are never expected to be seen again. **Unprofitable Streaming Services** : Despite being the primary revenue source for labels, streaming services like Spotify, Apple Music, and Google Play are unprofitable because licensing deals compel them to pay out over half of their income before any other expenses. **Upgraded Audio CDs (SACD/DVD-Audio)** : Certain CDs offer enhanced audio quality, such as specialized versions included in box sets (e.g., DVD versions) or the now less common Super Audio CDs (SACDs). These discs are produced with higher sampling rates and bit rates, often 96 kilohertz 24-bit, and are noted for their superior sound quality. "you can buy CDs that have upgraded audio, a DVD version that comes in a box set for example, or maybe a now hard to come by super audio CD." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B2g4QSREzpw

**Vinyl Manufacturing Quality Eras (Late 1970s - Early 1980s)** : The quality of vinyl record manufacturing significantly declined between the late 1970s and early 1980s. During this period, raw polyvinyl chloride (PVC) was often of poor quality, frequently recycled with impurities. Labels also reduced the amount of vinyl used per record, resulting in lighter discs (e.g., 90 grams) compared to the 180-gram vinyl commonly produced today. Consequently, a record manufactured in 1972 is likely to sound superior to one made in 1979. "When CDs first came out, there was no question that they sounded better than the vinyl records that were coming out at the time. That's because the manufacturing quality of vinyl in the late 70s and early 80s was unmitigated crap." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B2g4QSREzpw
**Vinyl Material Origin (1920s)** : Polyvinyl chloride, or vinyl, is a plastic polymer made from petroleum that was first commercially used in the 1920s for the manufacture of sewer pipes, later finding applications in electrical cables, clothing, and flooring. "The word vinyl is short for polyvinyl chloride, which chemist tell us is a plastic polymer made from petroleum that was first used in the 1920s for the manufacturer of sewer pipes." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=IIJySSo276k
**Vinyl Record Groove Length** : A standard 12-inch, 33 1/3 RPM vinyl album contains a spiral groove approximately 427 meters long, capable of storing about 20 minutes of music per side. "A standard 12-inch, 33-in-a-third, RPM vinyl album, features a spiral groove that runs about 427 meters, the store about 20 minutes worth of music." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=72Lei7uug8c
**Vinyl Record Origin Date (1948)** : The modern vinyl record format was introduced in 1948. "It dates back to 1948." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B2g4QSREzpw
**Vinyl Record Sizes** : Vinyl records are produced in a range of diameters, including common sizes such as 5, 7, 10, and 12 inches, with some even reaching 16 inches. Nine Inch Nails, for instance, famously released a record with a 9-inch diameter. "Vile can come in all sizes, 5 inches, 7 inches, 10 inches, 12 inches, even 16 inches" ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B2g4QSREzpw
**Vinyl Records Warping Due to High Temperatures (2021)** : In 2021, exceptionally high temperatures caused vinyl records to warp during transit. This issue affected records moving from warehouses to retail stores and even those shipped through postal services, highlighting the fragility of vinyl in extreme heat. "In fact, it was so hot that vinyl records were getting warped in transit from warehouses to record stores and even in the mail." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-N6IM5Nm5iw
**Vinyl Resurgence and Appeal** : Vinyl records have experienced an unexpected comeback, now outselling CDs in countries like Canada, the US, the UK, and Australia. Many consumers are purchasing vinyl even without a turntable, drawn to its tangible and physical nature as a representation of music. Fans often stream music while holding the vinyl, appreciating the physical ownership, liner notes, lyric sheets, and artwork. "Vinyl is once again outselling CDs and a surprising number of people are buying vinyl, even if they don't have a turntable." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sn5TWBFd0XM
**Vinyl Resurrection from Near Death** : Despite predictions in the late 1980s and early 1990s that vinyl was dying, with CDs dominating the market, vinyl experienced an improbable comeback. By 1999, new turntables were thought to be nearing obsolescence, and for the next 8-9 years, vinyl was on "life support" due to CDs, digital downloads, and piracy. However, around 2006-2007, indie bands began ironically embracing vinyl, and audiophiles continued to resist digital formats. This contrarian movement, combined with the inspiration of "Free Comic Book Day" leading to the creation of Record Store Day in 2008, sparked a consistent double-digit growth in vinyl sales, ultimately leading to vinyl outselling CDs in revenue and units in some countries by 2020, and in Canada by 2024 for the first time in 35 years. "around 2006 and 2007, indie bands started being contrarian and maybe ironic by embracing vinyl." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nX8uAFDzDYM
**Vinyl Sales Resurgence (Post-2008)** : Following the first Record Store Day in 2008, vinyl sales experienced consistent "double digit growth" year after year, globally. This boom was so significant that old pressing plants were brought back online, and new state-of-the-art manufacturing facilities were established from scratch to meet demand. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=eqTFinLk3oU
**Vinyl Sales as Niche Growth** : Vinyl sales represent the sole positive trend in physical music formats, though it remains a niche market when viewed within the broader music industry. "The only bright spot has been vinyl, which if we're honest, is just a niche in the grand scheme of things." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**Vinyl Sound Characteristics** : Vinyl recordings are particularly effective at preserving mid-range frequencies, which benefits sounds like "fuzzy rock guitars." However, the format can struggle with extremely high and low frequencies, where CDs excel. The perceived "warmth" of vinyl might be attributable to slight distortion in the bass frequencies. Additionally, sound quality on a vinyl record can degrade as the stylus moves closer to the center, where the smaller circumference and increased stylus speed make accurate groove tracing more difficult. "Vinyl recordings are very good at preserving the mid-range frequencies. That's where fuzzy rock guitars live, so they might sound better on vinyl to some people." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B2g4QSREzpw
**Vinyl Surpassing CD Sales (2020)** : In 2020, the dollar value of vinyl sold in the United States exceeded that of CDs, a phenomenon that had not occurred since 1986. This milestone underscored the remarkable resurgence of the vinyl format. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=eqTFinLk3oU
**Vinyl vs. CD Audio Quality Debate** : The debate over whether vinyl or compact discs offer superior audio quality is both scientific and emotional, influenced by subjective factors like emotional state and ear health. Technically, CDs are often superior, but perceived "warmth" and mastering differences create a complex listening experience. "To explain this is kind of difficult because the appreciation of audio quality is a very, very subjective thing that involves everything from your emotional state to the health of your ears." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=D8GU8ZpSgY8
**Vinyl's Decline and Resurgence (Early 2000s - Present)** : At the start of the 21st century, vinyl records were largely considered an obsolete format, viewed as dusty, scratchy, noisy, space-consuming, and prone to damage, especially in contrast to the then-emerging portable MP3s. Manufacturing significantly declined, with pressing plants closing. Only hardcore collectors and DJs maintained interest. However, from 2008 onwards, vinyl experienced a remarkable "zombie-like" resurgence, and by the 2020s, it began generating more revenue than compact discs due to premium pricing, despite ongoing challenges like supply chain issues and rising costs. "Starting in 2008, a weird thing happened. Like some zombie in one of those old Italian horror movies of the late 70s and early 80s, the format rose from the dead." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=B2g4QSREzpw
**Vision for New Tour: Music & Extreme Sports** : Kevin Lyman's goal for his new touring festival was to differentiate it by combining music with his passion for extreme sports, specifically skateboarding. "And the way he'd stand out is to pair music with his love of extreme sports, skateboarding specifically." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7jfQaUV6Bq4
**Vlad Drakuhl / Vlad the Impaler / Dracula Prototype** : Jay Parsons wrote his first-year university History 120 thesis paper on Vlad Drakuhl, also known as Vlad the Impaler, a brutal 15th-century tyrant from Transylvania and Romania. Vlad punished enemies by burning villages and impaling heads on stakes outside his castle to intimidate foes, a practice that Jay humorously likened to a "human kabab scenario." "I decided to write about Vlad Drakuhl, also known as Vlad the Impaler from Transylvania and Romania, 15th century. And he was a brutal tyrant." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=N-3eddy4J7g
**Vocables: Cultural and Historical Use** : Vocables are found in various musical traditions, including many indigenous songs and some Jewish melodies that consist solely of vocables. Historically, classical composers in the 15th century used specific vocables as filler words in their compositions, with "Dildo" being a notable example from the 1500s. "many indigenous songs use vocables. Some Jewish melodies are nothing but vocables." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_YjwRApoJhA
**Vocables: Definition** : Vocables are placeholder or nonsense sounds used in singing, such as "la la la," "na na na," or "doot doot doot." They do not carry any inherent meaning but are used for their phonetic or musical quality. "Those placeholders or nonsense sounds are called vocables. They don't have any meaning." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_YjwRApoJhA
**Vocables: Purpose and Audience Participation** : While vocables lack semantic meaning, they can convey feeling and emotion. They are particularly effective for audience participation, allowing listeners to sing along to a song even without lyrics, enhancing the communal concert experience. "sometimes a song screams for singing that doesn't say anything, doesn't mean anything, but feels good." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_YjwRApoJhA
**Vocal Adaptation Techniques for Aging Singers** : Older singers, whose voices change due to the deterioration of the larynx and vocal cords, often employ two primary strategies: modifying performances to avoid numerous high notes, or lowering the key of the song. Lowering the key transposes high notes to a more manageable range, allowing them to continue performing without damaging their voice. "some older singers will be forced to do one of two things. First, they'll change the performance so they avoid hitting too many high notes." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_YjwRApoJhA
**Vogue Magazine - 1972 Puffball Hairdo** : In 1972, David Bowie's wife, Angela, discovered a "puffball hairdo" in *Vogue* magazine. This hairstyle, with its distinctive moment-like profile, was adopted by Bowie and became incredibly trendy at the time, although it looks dated now. "His new wife, Angela, found a puffball hairdo in Vogue magazine." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=0xd0Hf5REg4
**Volkswagen "Milky Way" Ad Impact** : The Volkswagen ad featuring "Pink Moon" debuted in 2000, causing sales of Volkswagen cabriolets to jump, achieving its primary mission. Simultaneously, the commercial acted as a powerful sales driver for the song itself, demonstrating how a commercial could sell both a product and the music used in it. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sfie4YYncxk
**Volkswagen "Milky Way" Ad Turning Point** : The year 2000 marked a turning point in music licensing when the Boston-based agency Arnold World Wide created the "Milky Way" TV spot for Volkswagen's new cabriolet model, targeting 20-somethings who perceived VWs as "cooler" and "more underground." The romantic one-minute ad, featuring four hip young people driving in the countryside, sought a unique musical backdrop after testing songs from The Church, The Cure, and Tones on Tail, ultimately stumbling upon Nick Drake's obscure "Pink Moon." The choice was partly driven by budget and the desire for "indiness." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sfie4YYncxk
**Voyager Golden Disk** : Launched in 1977, the Voyager I and Voyager II spacecraft each carry a gold phonograph record called the "Voyager Golden Disk." This durable disk contains images, coordinates, drawings, sounds of Earth, spoken word recordings, and a significant amount of music, intended as an introduction to humanity for any advanced alien species that might discover the probes in interstellar space. "This is the Voyager Golden Disk. It's a phonograph record made of gold, one of the toughest elements in the universe." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=hQJ3r-DCoMQ
**Voyager Spacecraft Eight-Track Recorders** : Despite significant advancements in technology on Earth, the Voyager spacecraft, launched in 1977, remain technologically frozen in time. They were equipped with the best available technology of the era, including eight-track tape recorders for data storage, and have continued to function, maintaining a technological state forever fixed in 1977. "The two spacecraft are frozen, technologically speaking, because they were sent on their missions with the best equipment available at the time, including an eight-track tape recorder for data storage." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=GO0K_N7Ys7I
**Walkman's Impact on Music Sales and Copyright Debate** : The immense success of The Walkman led to a significant increase in sales for both pre-recorded cassettes and, notably, blank cassettes, which saw sales "go through the roof." This surge directly triggered "the crisis in home taping" and ignited widespread debate about individuals' rights to copy music from various sources. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z9T9wi0vEvc
**Walmart & Kmart – "Godsmack" Boycott (1998)** : In 1998, both Walmart and Kmart, major U.S. retail chains, refused to sell Godsmack's self-titled album due to complaints from a parent. The complaints cited the album's liner notes, which contained a Wiccan pentagram and references to "Salem Witches," as well as "outrageously offensive" lyrics and the absence of a parental advisory warning. "Both Walmart and Kmart wouldn't sell the record." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_tbKuJR0cPs
**Walter the Mathematician's Role** : Walter the Mathematician was enlisted to create a scale from 1 to 10 to assign a "power ranking" for each song's "one-hit wonder-ness." "I also enlisted Walter the Mathematician to create a scale from 1 to 10, which assigned each song a power ranking when it came to its one-hit wonder-ness." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZcF5JMhBj2M
**Warped Magazine as Name Origin** : The name "Warped" for the tour was adopted after Kevin Lyman decided against "The Bomb." The name came from a magazine he had previously worked on. "That's when he went with Warped, the name of a magazine he'd worked on in the past." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7jfQaUV6Bq4

This acetate contained unique recordings that preceded their debut album. "a guy named Warren Hill, a record collector from Montreal, stumbled across it at a flea market in the Chelsea neighborhood of New York." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=IIJySSo276k
**Website for Song Similarities** : For those interested in discovering songs that sound alike, the website SoundsJustLike.com is recommended. "If this is your thing, these days, you tunes, try a website called SoundsJustLike.com." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rfObVld9rIE
**Weezer – Rivers Cuomo's Childhood Asthma Nickname** : Rivers Cuomo had bad asthma as a kid and was called "Weezer" by friends. "Rivers Cuomo had bad asthma as a kid, so his friends called him Weezer." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NCQ1ERgSfeE
**Weezer's Roomba Merch – "Weezer Branded Robot Vacuum Cleaner" with Silent W** : Weezer offered a branded robot vacuum cleaner called a Roomba (with a silent W). They gave five away in a contest surrounding an album release. "They once offered a Weezer branded robot vacuum cleaner." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=ZbHRIh3RUoo
**Wernicke's Area and Music Appreciation** : Wernicke's area, positioned near Broca's area and the auditory cortex, is a region of the brain that plays a role in the analysis and enjoyment of music. "We use this part of the brain to analyze and enjoy music." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=mTbagQE7akQ
**Western Music Theory - 12-Note Scale** : Western music is fundamentally structured around "a scale of 12 notes." These notes are "related to each other mathematically by a series of ratios known as intervals of a semitone." "Western music is based on a scale of 12 notes." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rfObVld9rIE
**Western Music Theory - Building Blocks** : Combinations and patterns of the 12 notes in Western music reveal "chords, melodies, harmonies, and so on," which are considered "the building blocks of our music," from symphonies to pop songs. "Playing those notes in certain combinations or patterns reveal things like chords, melodies, harmonies, and so on." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=rfObVld9rIE
**Wet Leg – Random Emoji Combination on a Keyboard** : Rhian Teasdale and Hester Chambers found the name by randomly combining emojis together on a keyboard. **White Bell Bird's Loudness** : Scientists successfully measured the song of the white bell bird, determining it to be 125 decibels. This volume is louder than the PA systems at most rock concerts, equivalent to standing near a jet engine, and reaches the threshold of physical pain. "The answer is 125 decibels." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=72Lei7uug8c
**White Zombie – Rob Zombie's Love of a 1932 Bela Lugosi Horror Film** : Rob Zombie (real name Rob Cummings) named the band after a 1932 Bela Lugosi horror film called White Zombie. "One of his favorite was a 1932 Bela Lugosi movie called White Zombie." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NCQ1ERgSfeE
**Wholesale Devaluation of Music in the 21st Century** : The most significant shift in music this century is the wholesale devaluation of its monetary worth. In the 2000s, CDs cost $10-$20, requiring financial investment and careful selection by consumers who would then listen repeatedly. Today, music is an oversupplied product, effectively free or very cheap through streaming services offering millions of songs and 100,000 new uploads daily. This abundance leads to consumers skipping songs frequently (e.g., 25% skip unfamiliar songs in 5 seconds, 35% by 30 seconds), indicating a lack of financial investment, risk, and short attention spans. While music retains emotional and cultural value, its monetary worth has plummeted. Despite complaints about low artist payouts, many users utilize free tiers or pay minimal subscription fees, often unwilling to pay more to better compensate artists. This situation has made music a low-cost, low-margin commodity, impacting how and by whom music is made for the foreseeable future. "So this, the devaluation of music is my pick for the most important thing to happen to rock at all music so far in this century." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=nX8uAFDzDYM
**Why 33⅓ RPM? – Derived from Vitaphone Film Sync System; Math: 3,600 ÷ 108 = 33⅓** : The 33⅓ RPM speed was not arbitrary but derived from the Vitaphone film-sync system introduced in 1931 when movies first had synchronized sound. Film reels were 1,000 feet of 35mm film, good for 11 minutes of viewing. To make audio last exactly 11 minutes to match one film reel, the 3,600 RPM motor required a 108:1 gear ratio: 3,600 ÷ 108 = 33⅓. RCA tried to introduce this format to the public but the Great Depression killed consumer interest, and RCA foolishly let all the patents expire. Columbia Records later picked up the expired technology. "RCA let all the patents expire. Fast forward to the late 1940s." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=I291j5WBc4k
**Why 45 RPM and the Large 1.5-Inch Hole? – RCA's Revenge; Jukebox Torque Distribution** : When RCA discovered Columbia had beaten them using their own expired patents, they fought back with a 7-inch, 45 RPM single also made from PVC. The 45 RPM speed delivers a 35% increase in groove velocity compared to 33⅓, greatly reducing distortion and loss of high frequencies. The unusually large 1.5-inch center hole served two purposes: (1) RCA initially sold turntables that could only play 45s to lock consumers into their format; (2) the larger hole distributed the sudden rotational torque over a longer path (π × 1.5 ≈ 4.71 inches), preventing the hole from going out of round — crucial for the jukebox industry, which was enormous business in the 1940s–50s. "The larger hole allowed the sudden rotational force to be distributed over a longer path, pi times 1.5 or about 4.71 inches." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=I291j5WBc4k
**Why 78 RPM? – The Handbook Writer Just Had a Machine That Ran at That Speed** : The first turntable (the gramophone) was introduced in the early 1890s playing at "about 70 RPM." By the end of the 19th century a handbook for record manufacturers recommended the use of governors to standardize speed at 78 RPM. The reason that exact speed was chosen: whoever wrote the handbook had a machine that turned at that speed. The standard propulsion motors selected to power electric turntables ran at 3,600 RPM with a 46:1 gear ratio, yielding 78.26 RPM at 60 Hz — close enough. "The most likely reason is that whoever wrote the handbook had a machine that turned at that speed." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=I291j5WBc4k
**Widespread Adoption of Licensing Strategy** : Following successful campaigns by artists like Moby and Sting, thousands of other acts were inspired to pursue similar music licensing strategies. This led to a significant increase in demand for licensing deals across the industry. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sfie4YYncxk
**William Burroughs' Paintings** : Two paintings by the poet William Burroughs were owned by John Frusciante and were among the possessions lost in his house fire in 1996. "He lost all his paintings, including two that had been painted by poet William Burrows." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=TESrEzVHLCc
**William Morris Endeavor Agency** : The William Morris Endeavor agency provided the tour routing for Weezer's spring tour with The Pixies, which Rivers Cuomo demonstrated using his "Drive Times" app. "Here is the routing we got from Weezer's agent, William Morris, Endeavor agency." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=LYRPxtP61JM
**WinAMP and MP3 Compression (April 12, 1997)** : WinAMP, released as a free download on April 12, 1997, revolutionized digital music by enabling easy "ripping" of data from CDs to computer hard drives. Critically, it used the Motion Picture Expert's Group Layer 3 (MP3) technology, which compressed audio files significantly compared to giant wave files, allowing users to store a "lot more" music on their computers and facilitating early online file sharing. "WinAMP showed up as a free download on April the 12th, 1997 for all operating systems. WinAMP allowed anyone to easily transfer the data from a CD to a computer's hard drive." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NAroxFbYkRw
**Windows 95's Impact on Digital Music** : The "fat" nature of the Windows 95 operating system, requiring nearly 60 megabytes of hard drive space and 26 floppy disks for installation, led Bill Gates to bundle CD-ROM drives with PCs. This made CD-ROM drives standard, allowing users to realize they could transfer music data from audio CDs to their computers using programs like WinAMP and the MP3 format, initiating the digital music revolution. "We got here because the Windows 95 operating system was so fat." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NAroxFbYkRw
**Winnipeg's Obsession with Phantom of the Paradise** : The 1974 film "Phantom of the Paradise" was a complete box office flop everywhere in the world except Winnipeg, where it stayed in theaters for months and became an "essential part of the musical DNA" of the city. This local phenomenon is so ingrained that annual festivals are held, attracting stars from the movie. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=eqTFinLk3oU
**Wolf River Harbor Drowning Incident** : Wolf River Harbor in Memphis is connected to the Mississippi River and was the location where Jeff Buckley accidentally drowned on May 29, 1997. He had swum there before, but this time, he was fully clothed and wearing heavy boots, and a boat's wake led to his disappearance. "Wolf River Harbor, which is connected to the Mississippi River, listening to music. Jeff had gone swimming there a few times before, and decided to do it again." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=MmXDbXI6Sqk
**Wolfmother – Chosen at Last Second From a Tom Robbins Novel ("Skinny Legs")** : When the band showed up for their first gig without a name, bassist Chris Ross had been reading a Tom Robbins novel called Skinny Legs that contained the word "wolfmother." With no time left, they adopted it. Singer Andrew Stockdale and drummer Myles Heskett weren't crazy about it but accepted it on the spot. "With time running out before they were due on stage, they said, oh, okay, we're Wolfmother." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=QkYHaJKohVQ
**Working as a Musician in the 21st Century** : Being a musician in the 21st century requires a strong focus on business issues due to the changing economy, which differs significantly from previous eras where selling records and playing gigs sufficed. Multiple revenue sources, including sales, streaming, record labels, patrons, crowdfunding, merchandise, and licensing, are now necessary for survival. ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=sfie4YYncxk
**World War II Shellac Crisis and Record Shortage (1940s)** : The Japanese invasion of the Malay Peninsula on December 8, 1941, severely impacted the recording industry by cutting off the world's primary supply of shellac. Shellac, derived from the waxy resin secreted by female lac bugs found in the region (modern-day Malaysia, Thailand, Myanmar, Singapore), was a crucial component (one-third) of phonograph records in the 1940s, alongside minerals, cotton fibers, and carbon. This interruption led to a significant record shortage, forcing consumers to trade in old records to acquire new ones. "But because the Meile Peninsula was now under hostile control by the Japanese Imperial Army, the world's supply of raw materials for shellac, and therefore records, was shut down." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_Pi7JLwAeOI
**World's Biggest Record Show (Utrecht)** : The world's largest record show takes place every November in Utrecht, Netherlands. This event features at least 500 dealers spread across a giant convention hall over a two-day period. "The World's biggest record show was held every November in Utrecht in the Netherlands. At least 500 dealers set up for two days inside a giant convention hall." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=aSk1XQHNkd8
**XWA Montreal – First Licensed North American Radio Station; First Entertainment Broadcast May 20, 1920** : The first licensed radio station in North America was XWA (the X standing for "experimental"), run by the Marconi Wireless Telegraph Company of Canada. Voice transmission tests began in spring 1919 using a 500-watt transmitter at 173 Williams Street in Montreal; bored engineers started grabbing phonograph records from the nearby Layton Brothers record store to play instead of reciting the alphabet — giving Layton Brothers an inadvertent claim as radio's first advertiser. The first documented entertainment broadcast for the general public occurred on May 20, 1920, when soprano Dorothy Hutton sang two ballads from the XWA studio. KDKA in Pittsburgh — often cited by Americans as the first radio station — did not sign on until November 2, 1926. "KDKA didn't go on the air until November 2, 1926 — later." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=JvOEAzmC6l0
**Year 2000 as Music Industry Divide** : The year 2000 is identified as a clear dividing line, distinguishing between artists who rose to prominence in the pre-internet era and those who emerged in the post-internet landscape. "The year 2000 is a nice, neat division between pre-internet and post-internet artists." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**You Hear Me** : You Hear Me is a mental health organization that addresses issues of bullying and peer pressure, and it was among the causes supported by the Warped Tour. "And you hear me as another mental health organization that also deals with bullying and peer pressure." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7jfQaUV6Bq4
**Youth as Drivers of Music and Society** : Music is consistently driven by youth and is always downstream from societal trends. Young people, especially with access to vast musical libraries today, absorb diverse influences, which will ultimately lead them to create new music that could "change the world in a way that we can't even comprehend." "Music is always, always driven by youth and music is always downstream from society. So whatever is happening in society, we'll get into music because of youth." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=4xxgPX-aR90
**Yurgen Volmer's Origin of Beatles Haircut** : Yurgen Volmer, a friend of Astrid Kircher and Stu Sutcliffe, met John Lennon and Paul McCartney in Paris in 1961. Volmer wore his hair long and over his forehead, a style that "entranced" Lennon and McCartney, who asked him to cut their hair this way, initiating the Beatles' iconic mop top look. "There is some photographic evidence to suggest that Volmer had been wearing his hair long and in a part, something that Paul described as a long-haired Hitler thing." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=VnXBJqKwkrw
**Zero Freitas's Record Collection** : Zero Freitas, a collector from Brazil, embarked on a mission to amass a private collection encompassing every record ever made, accumulating at least 8 million records and over 100,000 compact discs, though even this vast collection doesn't cover all music. "He has at least 8 million records and more than 100,000 compact discs, which is nice, but it still doesn't cover everything." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**Ziggy Stardust's Cultural Legacy** : Beyond music, David Bowie's Ziggy Stardust character had a profound cultural impact, inspiring books, PhD theses, and even having a hotel in Thailand and a village in Cyprus named after him. Ziggy also influenced subcultures like Goths and future industrial stars, making him arguably the "most famous and influential fictional rock star ever conceived." "Ziggy Stardust was quite simply the most famous and influential fictional rock star ever conceived." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=2RKchFW42Jc
**Zoroastrianism (Religion)** : The ancient religion of Zoroastrianism is referenced in 21 Pilots' *Trench* album mythology through the city of Dema. In Zoroastrianism, Dema refers to a "tower of silence," a structure where followers traditionally placed dead bodies to be consumed by vultures. "It describes a world built around the city of dma, which translated from the religion of Zoroastrianism, means tower of silence." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=8sAxFvktx-A
**iTunes Music Store Launch (2003)** : The iTunes Music Store debuted in 2003, initially offering hundreds of thousands of songs for sale, and has since grown to include approximately 60 million purchasable tracks. "Then in 2003 came the iTunes music star." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**iTunes Music Store and Legal Single Purchases** : The introduction of the iTunes Music Store on January 9th, 2001, dealt another "big stake through the heart" of the album era, as it allowed consumers to legitimately and legally purchase individual tracks instead of full albums, leading to a decline in album sales. "Another big stake through the heart was the introduction of the iTunes music store on January 9th, 2001." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Z7glrAoXtkA
**iTunes Music Store and the Return of the Single (2003)** : The launch of the iTunes Music Store in 2003 brought about the resurgence of the single, as albums were dismantled and individual tracks became available for purchase at 99 cents each, significantly impacting the traditional album concept. "Then along came the iTunes music store in 2003. Albums were broken up and sold as individual tracks for 99 cents." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Sc9HApnyBLM
**iTunes Software Deterioration** : Introduced on January 9, 2001, after Apple acquired Sound Jam, iTunes software began as a simple and functional tool. However, "over the years, though, it has become bloated and dysfunctional and tasked with doing too many things." It struggles as a combined music store, iPhone/iPad/iPod device manager, often exhibiting buggy syncing, archaic library management, high memory consumption, slow performance, difficulty with large libraries (leading to seizing or crashing), and lack of support for high-resolution audio files like FLAQ, performing "awful on Windows machines." "Over the years, though, it has become bloated and dysfunctional and tasked with doing too many things." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7Zniri9uSlc
**iTunes and Album Sales** : The rise of iTunes contributed to a shift in music consumption habits, with fans becoming accustomed to buying single songs rather than full albums, which prompted record labels to promote concept albums as a way to encourage full album purchases. "Itunes and downloading. See fans were getting far too used to the idea of being able to just buy one song at a time and not buying full albums." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=1KyNY3zv6uc
**iTunes and Streaming Saving the Music Industry** : After Napster caused CD sales to plummet, Steve Jobs' iTunes Music Store offered the record labels a legitimate digital download option, quickly dominating the market. Subsequently, the rise of streaming services, starting with Rhapsody in 2001 and led by Spotify, became the primary revenue source for labels (65-80% today), effectively "saving the music industry" from the brink of collapse caused by illegal file sharing. "Streaming saved the music industry." ← https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=NAroxFbYkRw
